STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION AND ANALYSIS



1.               INTRODUCTION
1.1              Project Description
The Kyrgyz Republic is a landlocked, lower-middle-income country with rich natural endowments, including minerals,
forests, arable land and pastures, and significant potential for expansion of agriculture, hydroelectricity production, and
tourism.
In October 2021, the Cabinet of Ministers of Kyrgyz Republic (the Government) launched a medium-term development
program for 2021 to 2026 with the goal of “leaving no one behind.” The priority areas of the program include anti-crisis
measures; management reform; establishment of an enabling environment for development; development of key
sectors of the economy, including energy; social development; foreign policy and national security; and special
priorities. Energy is presented as a key sector to be reformed, including through gradual tariff adjustments toward cost
recovery, exploiting the country’s renewable energy potential, modernization of sector assets, and transition to the
wholesale electricity market.
In the recently prepared Energy White Paper that lays out the pillars of reform direction, the Government has set
ambitious targets for renewable energy deployment. Under the third pillar of the white paper, the Government seeks
to procure 700 megawatts of solar capacity and as much as 100 megawatts of small hydropower plant capacity by the
end of 2030, and it has accelerated the preparation of large HPPs such as Kambarata-1, which would take a decade to
develop and construct, to support the ambitious transformation of the energy sector.
The proposed Kambarata-1 Hydro Power Plant Sustainable and Transformational Energy Program (K1-STEP) supports
the Government and Central Asia countries to sustain strong economic growth, enhance energy security and advance
the clean energy transition. The K1-STEP could play a major role in meeting the country’s renewable energy targets,
facilitating renewable energy integration, and mitigating climate change impacts.
The Kambarata-1 Hydropower Plant (K1-HPP) is a transformational greenfield clean energy project with substantial
domestic and regional benefits aimed at bolstering energy security, accelerating the clean energy transition, improving
water management and strengthening the regional cooperation in Central Asia. The proposed plant’s installed capacity
of 1,880 megawatts could reach an average annual generation of 5,961 gigawatt hours, which is equivalent to 1,073
megawatts of coal-/gas-fired power plants operating at a 60 percent capacity factor. It is estimated to avoid emission
of about 4.8 million tons of CO2 per year from coal plants or about 2.5 million tons of CO2 from gas power plants.
The K1-STEP could also address the country’s energy security challenge, meeting export obligations and realizing export
revenues, while improving downstream HPP operations and water management. The development of renewables will
greatly reduce the country’s reliance on imported fossil fuels in winter while providing the country with additional
export revenue.
The K1-STEP is being prepared under the World Bank’s Environment and Social Framework (ESF).
In line with the World Bank Environmental and Social Framework (ESF), and specifically Environmental and Social
Standard 10 (ESS10) on Stakeholder Engagement and Information Disclosure, the preparation of the Kambarata-1
Hydropower Project includes structured stakeholder engagement and public information disclosure processes.
This Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) has been prepared to document consultations conducted to date, identify
relevant stakeholders, and define principles and procedures for information disclosure and stakeholder engagement
during all project phases. The SEP will be updated periodically, as needed

.

1.2              Project Components
The K1-STEP Program is designed in three phases with indicative total funding envelope of US$1,500 million, including
the Phase 1 – US$500 million, from the World Bank to the Kyrgyz Republic, the Republic of Uzbekistan, and the Republic
of Kazakhstan.
Phase 1 of the K1-STEP Program (the Project) will consist of the following components.
      1.   Component 1. Construction works. This component will finance the early works such as diversion tunnels, dam,
           powerhouse and substation pre-excavation, site installation. It will also finance main civil works, including the
           mobilization of main civil works contractor, plus the equipment contract, including basic design, four turbines
           plus additional downpayment to book manufacturing facilities for main equipment.
      2.   Component 2. Project institutional setup, design, management and supervision. This component will finance
           the Project Management Consultancy (PMC), project implementation support, and the operationalization of
           the Kambarata-1 Regional JV.
                a.    Subcomponent 1. Operationalization of the Kambarata-1 Regional project company (JV).
                b.    Subcomponent 2. Project Management Consultancy (PMC).
                c.    Subcomponent 3. Project implementation support.
                d.    Subcomponent 4. Owner’s cost.
      3.   Component 3. Implementation of social and environmental management plans. This component will finance
           the implementation and monitoring of E&S instruments including ESMP, Livelihood Restoration and
           Resettlement Framework (LRRF) requirements. As a complement to environment and social risk mitigation
           investments, a Benefit Sharing Program would be implemented through a dedicated Fund to be financed
           through the revenue of the Project.
      4.   Component 4. Water management institutional capacity and regional coordination support. This component
           will finance a set of hydro meteorological instrumentation and consultancy services required for the
           Kambarata-1 Hydro Power Plant (HPP) project, including key monitoring investments in the upper Naryn river
           basin to support the operation of Kambarata-1 HPP and mitigate future climate risks, and improve the water
           management institutional foundations within the Kambarata-1 HPP project and broader regional framework.
The K1-HPP was developed back in the Soviet years. As planned, the K1-HPP will become the largest hydroelectric power
station in Kyrgyz Republic with 1,880 megawatts. For comparison: the capacity of the Toktogul HPP is 1,200 megawatts.
It will create a reservoir of around 62 km2. It is located some 14 km upstream of the existing Kambarata-2 Hydropower
Plant (HPP) in a gorge where the Naryn River makes a large loop around the Karabash Mountain (see Figure 1-1 and
Figure 1-2).

Figure 1-2: Components of the K1-HPP Dam

The project components described above define the scope, sequencing, and potential environmental and social
interactions of the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Project. These components also inform the identification of relevant
stakeholders and the timing of stakeholder engagement activities under this Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP).
Stakeholder engagement at the current project preparation and disclosure stage focuses on information disclosure and
consultation related to the ESIA process, while engagement approaches applicable to subsequent ESIA updates and
later stages of project development, including construction, operation, and the implementation of environmental and
social management measures, will be further detailed prior to those stages. Details will be defined at a later stage and
reflected in updated environmental and social management instruments.

1.3              Project Environmental and Social Risks/Impacts
Potential environmental risks, related to the K1-HPP, include permanent inundation of the reservoir area and changes
in landscapes; alteration of flow in some stretches of the Naryn River, its water quality, and hydromorphology, including
sediment transport; and changes in terrestrial and aquatic biodiversity in ecosystems dependent on it.
The works and inundation of the reservoir area are expected to have potential adverse risks and impacts on both
terrestrial and aquatic ecosystems and biodiversity, including potential cumulative impacts and transboundary impacts.

STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION AND ANALYSIS

The variety of impacts of identified project activities, which include irreversible impacts as well as those that would alter
the natural flows and terrain significantly, augmented further by the need for more robust management due to the
complexities associated with project impacts and implementation, even with the current familiarity and experience of
the Borrower with ESF requirements, have led to environmental risk being rated as high.
Stakeholder comments related to cumulative impacts, biodiversity, and transboundary issues are addressed through
the Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) and Cumulative Impact Assessment (CIA) processes and are
documented separately in the ESIA disclosure package and the Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP). This Stakeholder
Engagement Plan focuses on information disclosure, consultation, and engagement processes and does not duplicate
technical impact assessment or mitigation measures addressed under other environmental and social instruments.
The envisioned social risks of the project refer to limited stakeholder engagement, project information disclosure, and
the need for an effective grievance mechanism for handling complaints. Labor influx is expected to be significant for
around 8.5 years, with a peak of 7,000 workers, creating challenges for this low-density area.
Also, even if the initial social screening has shown that there are only a few permanent human settlements in the dam
site and impounded area, every year, shepherd families move with their herds from villages to the pastures located in
the project area.
Because of it, the project would likely cause the displacement of some structures used for seasonal animal husbandry
and temporary residence, limit the use and access to some seasonal pastures, potential economic displacement due to
more limited of access to pasturelands for communities living around the project area impacting local livelihood. For
the above reasons, the social risks have been classified as substantial.
A total of 10 villages will be impacted by the Project as indicated in the table below.

Given the high environmental risk rating and substantial social risk classification, effective stakeholder engagement and
information disclosure are critical elements of project preparation. This Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) has been
developed in accordance with the World Bank Environmental and Social Standard 10 (ESS10) to ensure that potentially
affected communities and other stakeholders are informed of the project, understand potential risks and impacts, and
have opportunities to raise concerns and provide feedback during the assessment stage.
At subsequent stages of the project, and subject to ESIA updates, project design finalization, contractor mobilization,
and institutional arrangements, stakeholder engagement activities will be further structured and operationalized. The
stakeholder engagement methodology applicable to subsequent ESIA updates and later stages of project development,

including mechanisms for ongoing consultation, feedback integration, and coordination with environmental and social
management instruments, is outlined in later sections of this SEP.
In line with the World Bank Environmental and Social Framework (ESF), the Project also triggers Environmental and
Social Standard 5 (ESS5) on Land Acquisition, Restrictions on Land Use and Involuntary Resettlement.
While detailed resettlement and livelihood restoration measures will be defined at later stages of project preparation,
the Project has prepared a Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Framework (LRRF) to establish the core principles,
eligibility logic, and procedural approach applicable to potential land acquisition, restrictions on land use, and economic
or physical displacement associated with the Project.
At the current disclosure stage, potential social risks under ESS5 are primarily associated with:
          (i) restrictions on access to seasonal pastures and natural resources;
          (ii) potential economic displacement of households reliant on livestock and seasonal land use;
          (iii) displacement of temporary or seasonal structures used for animal husbandry or temporary residence.
The LRRF applies to all categories of potentially affected persons, including those with formal legal rights, customary or
traditional use rights, and those without formal land titles, in accordance with ESS5. The framework establishes the
principle that livelihood restoration measures will be designed to restore, and where feasible improve, the livelihoods
and living standards of affected persons to pre-project levels.


The LRRF defines that eligibility for assistance will be determined based on a cut-off date to be established and
communicated during subsequent census and socio-economic surveys, to be conducted as part of the preparation of
site-specific Resettlement Action Plans (RAPs) and/or Livelihood Restoration Plans (LRPs).
Institutional responsibilities, detailed compensation measures, livelihood restoration programs, implementation
schedules, and monitoring arrangements will be further developed through RAPs and LRPs, consistent with ESS5, and
reflected in the Environmental and Social Commitment Plan (ESCP).

1.4              Purpose and Objectives of the Stakeholder Engagement Plan
The overall objective of this SEP is to:
      •   Define a program for stakeholder engagement, including public information disclosure, grievance redress
          mechanism and consultation, throughout the implementation and operation of the proposed Project.
      •   Outline the ways in which K1-PMO and contractors will communicate with stakeholders and include a
          mechanism by which people can raise concerns, provide feedback, or make complaints about the project,
          contractors, and the Project itself.
The consultation and engagement with the local population is essential to the success of the Project in order to ensure
smooth collaboration between project staff and local communities and to minimize and mitigate environmental and
social risks related to the proposed Project.
The overall objective of this SEP is to define a structured approach for stakeholder engagement, including public
information disclosure, consultation, and grievance redress arrangements, during the project preparation and
assessment stage of the proposed Project, in accordance with the World Bank Environmental and Social Standard 10
(ESS10).

understandable, accessible, and appropriate manner and format, provide citizens with accessible and inclusive means
to raise issues and grievances and enable the project implementing entity to respond to and manage such grievances

consultations, and grievance arrangements applicable at this stage.

entitlements, livelihood restoration, and community benefit measures will be further defined and operationalized at
subsequent project stages. An updated stakeholder engagement methodology will be prepared prior to construction,
aligned with finalized project design, contractor mobilization, and approved environmental and social management
instruments.

grievance arrangements during the project preparation and assessment stage. It does not define compensation
entitlements, livelihood restoration measures, or resettlement implementation arrangements, which are addressed
under ESS5 through the Livelihood Restoration and Resettlement Framework (LRRF) and subsequent site-specific
instruments.
The SEP ensures that stakeholders are informed about ESS5-related processes, including land access restrictions,
eligibility principles, and grievance mechanisms, and that stakeholder feedback related to land acquisition and livelihood
impacts is documented and considered through the environmental and social assessment process.

1.5              Beneficiaries
Beneficiaries of the K1-STEP are the electricity consumers, including industrial, commercial, and residential customers.
The newly constructed hydropower plant will help increase power supply in the Kyrgyz Republic and improve the quality
of power in the country In addition, the local population may benefit indirectly from employment opportunities
associated with project preparation and subsequent project stages, subject to applicable labor and procurement
arrangements.
The implementing agencies will also benefit significantly from the capacity building and technical assistance they will
receive, which will improve their capacity to plan, develop, manage and maintain existing hydropower systems and
services. Consequently, they will be able to deliver better, more reliable, and cost-effective services to customers.

1.6              Non-Beneficiary Stakeholders

In addition to direct project beneficiaries, the Project has identified a range of non-beneficiary stakeholders who may
have an interest in, or provide relevant perspectives on, the environmental and social aspects of the Kambarata-1
Hydropower Project. These stakeholders include non-governmental organizations (NGOs), academic and research
institutions, local institutes (including vocational and training institutions), multilateral and development partners,
media representatives, and organized civil-society groups, including women’s and youth organizations.
Engagement with non-beneficiary stakeholders during the project preparation stage is intended to support information
disclosure, transparency, and the exchange of views on potential environmental and social risks and impacts, in line
with ESS10. These stakeholders may contribute technical, institutional, or thematic insights related to biodiversity
conservation, social inclusion, labor issues, cultural heritage, and community well-being.
The roles, formats, and frequency of engagement with non-beneficiary stakeholders applicable to subsequent stages of
the Project, including construction and operation, will be further defined in line with future ESIA updates, project design
finalization, and institutional arrangements, prior to those stages. This SEP does not assign implementation-stage
responsibilities or commitments to non-beneficiary stakeholders.
This Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) is a living document and will be updated as the Project progresses. Stakeholder
comments received during ESIA preparation, including those related to cumulative and transboundary impacts, are
documented and addressed through formal response mechanisms and relevant environmental and social instruments.
The SEP focuses on ensuring transparent, inclusive, and safe engagement processes, without duplicating or substituting
technical mitigation measures addressed under other project instruments.
In addition, where transboundary considerations are relevant, non-government stakeholders in potentially affected
riparian countries (such as civil society organizations, research institutions, water user associations, and media) may be
engaged through appropriate disclosure and consultation formats, consistent with ESS10 and applicable national
procedures. Detailed transboundary engagement arrangements are further described in Chapter 5.
Any expansion of stakeholder engagement activities to address cumulative or transboundary matters will be undertaken
in coordination with the applicable legal, institutional, and financing arrangements.

2.               REGULATIONS AND REQUIREMENTS
2.1              Kyrgyz Republic Requirements
This SEP considers the national legal and institutional framework of the Kyrgyz Republic relevant to stakeholder
engagement, public consultation, access to information, and grievance handling. The SEP is designed to align with and
complement these national requirements while meeting the World Bank ESS10 provisions. Constitution of the Kyrgyz
Republic (May 5, 2021): The Constitution guarantees the right of citizens to participate in discussions and decisions of
national and local importance, allowing citizens to participate in legislative and political processes. The Constitution
protects the rights of people with disabilities, the elderly and other vulnerable groups and ensures the functioning of a
social protection system that promotes the full and equal exercise of human and civil rights. It aims to enable social
integration without discrimination and the creation of an accessible environment for people with disabilities
Constitution of the Kyrgyz Republic (May 5, 2021): Key principles relevant to ESS10 include: (i) the right to participate in
matters of national and local importance; (ii) the right to seek and receive information held by public authorities; and
(iii) the protection of vulnerable groups and non-discrimination, including accessibility considerations for persons with
disabilities and elderly people.
The Law of the Kyrgyz Republic "On the Procedure for Considering Citizens’ Appeals" (May 4, 2007, No. 67, ). In the
Kyrgyz Republic, every citizen has the right to apply personally or through a representative to state bodies, local
government bodies and their officials, who are obliged to give a reasoned response within the time period established
by law.
The law establishes requirements for registration, fair consideration, and provision of a reasoned response within
statutory timeframes.
Law of the Kyrgyz Republic “On the Fundamentals of Administrative Activities and Administrative Procedures” (July
31, 2015, No. 210). This law governs administrative decision-making and procedures, including transparency
requirements and the handling of complaints related to administrative acts (including refusals related to access to
information). It also clarifies that the Law “On the Procedure for Considering Citizens’ Appeals” applies to appeals not
related to administrative procedures (Part 4 of Article 96).


According to Article 115 of the Administrative Procedure Code of the Kyrgyz Republic, the court may return the claim
if the plaintiff has not followed the legally established pre-trial dispute resolution procedure, and the possibility of using
this procedure has not been lost. This means that before going to court, the citizen must go through all the prescribed
stages of pre-trial resolution, including appealing to the state authorities (in this case, the Ministry of Energy) and
receiving an official response.
Law of the Kyrgyz Republic “On the right of access to information” of the Kyrgyz Republic of (December 29, 2023
No.217). The law contains provisions aimed at implementing and protecting the right of everyone to access information
held by information holders, including government agencies and local governments. It is aimed at achieving maximum
information openness, transparency and accountability in their activities, including through the form of open data
(access to information via the Internet, Open Government). The law provides for the disclosure of information on the
activities of government agencies, current decisions and official events, ensuring access to documents, etc.

The law supports proactive disclosure and access to official information held by public bodies, including through online
channels.
Law "On Regulatory Legal Acts" (July 20, 2009, No. 24). This law provides a framework for public discussion of draft
normative legal acts affecting the interests of citizens and legal entities by posting them on the official website of the
rule-making body. Draft normative legal acts of local government bodies may be posted in special places determined
by the representative body of local government.
According to Article 22, draft regulatory legal acts that directly affect the interests of citizens and legal entities, as well
as regulate entrepreneurial activity, are subject to public discussion by posting on the official website of the rule-making
body. Draft regulatory legal acts of representative bodies of local self-government may be posted in special places
(stands, stands) determined by the representative body of local self-government.
The regulatory body discloses contact information (address, including e-mail address, telephone numbers for receiving
fax messages), as well as the last name, first name, patronymic and contact information of the person responsible for
receiving proposals and comments, together with the draft regulatory act.
Regulations on local state administration and local government bodies (October 20, 2021, No. 123). According to
Article 7, Local self-government is carried out in the form of representative and direct participation of the local
community in local self-government. One of the forms of participation of the local community in the implementation of
local self-government is the discussion of all important issues of public and local significance at meetings (gatherings)
of members of the local community.
Citizens' meetings (gatherings) are a direct form of population participation in resolving issues of local importance by
discussing them at meetings of citizens living on the territory of one street, one block or village, with the adoption of
recommendations or decisions on them in accordance with the charter of the local community.
In order to take into account, the opinion of members of the local community on important issues, meetings (gatherings)
and public hearings are held with the participation of members of the local community with the adoption of
recommendations on them. The recommendations of meetings (gatherings) are considered with the participation of
representatives (delegates) of the relevant meetings (gatherings) (Article 63).
Citizens have the right to contact local government bodies and their officials, as well as to receive information about
the activities of local government bodies in accordance with the law. The local government administration of the district
is obliged to ensure interaction with civil society and the media.
Law of the Kyrgyz Republic "On Personal Information" dated April 14, 2008, No. 58. The law is aimed at legal regulation
of personal data on the basis of generally accepted international principles and norms, the Constitution of the Kyrgyz
Republic and in order to ensure the protection of human rights and freedoms associated with the collection, processing
and use of personal data.
Law "On Environmental Expertise" (June 16, 1999, No. 54): This law requires that public opinion be taken into account
when conducting environmental assessments. The purpose of public consultations is to inform the public about planned
activities, identify alternatives and determine all aspects of possible environmental impacts in order to obtain the most
objective information.
According to Article 4 of the Law on Environmental Expertise, one of the principles is taking into account public opinion.

     1) project initiator;
     2) the contractor for the EIA work;

Order of the State Agency for Architecture, Construction and Housing and Public Utilities under the Government of the
Kyrgyz Republic dated January 24, 2018 No. 12-NZA. The Regulation states the mandatory consideration of the opinion
of the population when developing and approving urban planning documentation by the state body for architecture,
construction and housing and public utilities. Local government bodies are also recommended to be guided by this
Regulation when conducting public hearings on urban planning documentation.
The regulation establishes the procedure for holding public hearings for the purpose of discussing urban planning
documentation in the form of draft general plans, detailed plans for districts, planning blocks, urban planning units, and
engineering and transport communications schemes. Participants in public hearings are: the interested public; public
organizations and associations; the state administration of the district, the mayor's office of the city of republican or
regional significance, state bodies whose competence includes making the decisions under discussion; the media.
Law of the Kyrgyz Republic "On Electronic Management" dated July 19, 2017 No. 127. The law applies to relations on
the provision of state and municipal services in electronic form using the state infrastructure of electronic governance,
as well as to the activities of organizations and citizens in making legally significant decisions and performing legally
significant actions using electronic documents and other information in electronic form.
Regulation on the management of public investments (Resolution of the Government of the Kyrgyz Republic dated
May 28, 2019 No. 232). The Regulation on the Management of Public Investments regulates the uniform procedure for
initiating, selecting, preparing, implementing, monitoring and evaluating public investment projects and applies to state
bodies and local governments. It was adopted to ensure high quality of preparation and implementation of public
investment projects in order to obtain the maximum possible social and economic return from budget investments. The
executing agency discusses the project documentation or feasibility study with all interested organizations and project
beneficiaries. The results of this discussion are reflected in the project documentation or feasibility study. The executing
agency is responsible for organizing such discussions.

where the exercise of powers of a state body requires obtaining information, opinions, and examinations from other

The stakeholder engagement and grievance mechanisms described in this SEP are designed to operate in a manner

documented responses consistent with national procedures.

submissions, where feasible) are intended to complement national administrative and judicial complaint mechanisms.
They do not replace statutory procedures under the Law “On the Procedure for Considering Citizens’ Appeals” and the
Administrative Procedures framework, nor do they limit the right of stakeholders to seek redress through courts or
competent authorities.

Kyrgyz Republic “On Personal Information” (2008, No. 58), including limitations on disclosure of complainant identity
and controlled access to personal data.

2.2              International Bodies and Conventions
Both the Kyrgyz Republic and the Republic of Kazakhstan are Parties to two key international environmental
agreements: the Convention on Environmental Impact Assessment in a Transboundary Context (Espoo Convention,
1991) and the Convention on Access to Information, Public Participation in Decision-Making and Access to Justice in
Environmental Matters (Aarhus Convention, 1998). Uzbekistan and Tajikistan are also parties to the Aarhus convention
but not to the Espoo convention.
The Espoo Convention requires that countries assess the environmental impacts of certain activities at an early stage of
planning and notify and consult with any Party which it considers may be an affected Party when a proposed activity is
likely to have a significant adverse transboundary environmental impact. This includes provisions for:
      •   Early notification to potentially affected Parties;
      •   Public participation in both the originating and affected Parties;
      •   Environmental impact assessment procedures aligned with international standards;
      •   Consultation mechanisms between states to avoid or mitigate transboundary harm.
This framework is particularly relevant in projects involving shared water resources, energy infrastructure, or activities
near international borders such as the K1-HPP, ensuring that neighbouring countries are informed and engaged in a
timely manner and consistent with applicable procedures for transboundary information sharing and participation..
The Aarhus Convention complements the Espoo Convention by operationalizing three key pillars:
      •   Access to Environmental Information – the public must be able to obtain timely and relevant environmental
          information held by public authorities;

meetings, participant attendance, issues raised, and responses provided. This documentation will be included in the
ESIA and its annexes, in accordance with ESS10 requirements.
Monitoring and reporting arrangements related to stakeholder engagement during project implementation will be
defined at subsequent stages.
This consultation was conducted in an intergovernmental / technical format. Stakeholder engagement with non-
government stakeholders in riparian countries (CSOs, academia, media, user associations) will be planned as part of the
updated SEP engagement program, using accessible disclosure channels and hybrid consultation formats. Records
(participants, issues, and responses) will be documented and included in the CRM.
STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION AND ANALYSIS

      •   Public Participation in Environmental Decision-Making – authorities must provide opportunities for public input
          during key stages of decision-making processes, particularly those with environmental implications;
      •   Access to Justice – the public must have access to judicial or administrative procedures to challenge public
          decisions that have been made without proper access to information or participation.
These conventions not only establish legal obligations but also serve as normative benchmarks for best practices in
stakeholder engagement, particularly in ESIA processes. By adhering to the principles embedded in these conventions,
the project aims to ensure transparency, build trust with affected communities, and proactively manage cross-border
environmental and social risks.
The procedures established under the Espoo and Aarhus Conventions are led and implemented by the competent
national authorities of the Kyrgyz Republic in coordination with relevant counterpart institutions in potentially affected
countries. This Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) does not replace or replicate formal transboundary notification or
consultation procedures but documents how principles of transparency, access to information, and public participation
are reflected in the project’s environmental and social assessment process, in alignment with these international
frameworks.
Transboundary notification and consultation under the Espoo Convention, where applicable, are led by the competent
national authorities of the Kyrgyz Republic in coordination with counterpart institutions in potentially affected
countries. The SEP does not replace formal Espoo procedures; it documents how transparency, access to information,
and public participation principles are reflected in Project-level disclosure and consultation activities, consistent with
institutional arrangements.
In line with the Aarhus Convention, principles of transparency and inclusive access to environmental information are
being applied during project preparation. Environmental and social documentation, including the ESIA and SEP, is
disclosed through official government channels in accordance with national procedures. Opportunities for participation
by stakeholders from potentially affected riparian countries will be facilitated through accessible disclosure and
consultation formats, subject to applicable institutional arrangements and coordination led by competent authorities.
Project-level disclosure and consultation activities documented in this SEP are complementary to, and do not substitute
for, formal transboundary procedures led by competent authorities. Project-level disclosure and consultation activities
documented in this SEP are complementary to, and do not substitute for, formal transboundary procedures led by
competent authorities.

2.3              World Bank requirements for stakeholder engagement
The World Bank’s Environmental and Social Framework (ESF)’s Environmental and Social Standard (ESS) 10,
“Stakeholder Engagement and Information Disclosure”, recognizes “the importance of open and transparent
engagement between the Borrower and project stakeholders as an essential element of good international practice”
In accordance with ESS10, a Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) is required to be prepared to guide stakeholder
engagement and information disclosure for projects supported by the World Bank.
Scope of application. ESS10 applies to all projects supported by the Bank through Investment Project Financing. For the
purpose of this ESS, “stakeholder” refers to individuals or groups who: (a) are affected or likely to be affected by the
project (project-affected parties); and (b) may have an interest in the project (other interested parties).
The Bank standard on Stakeholder Engagement and Information Disclosure requires that the project implementing
agency engages with stakeholders throughout the project life cycle, commencing such engagement as early as possible
in the project development process and in a timeframe that enables meaningful consultations with stakeholders on

project design. The nature, scope and frequency of stakeholder engagement will be proportionate to the nature and
scale of the project and its potential risks and impacts.
In line with ESS10 requirements, the project implementing arrangements provide meaningful consultations with
stakeholders, including the provision of timely, relevant, understandable and accessible information, and culturally
appropriate engagement free of manipulation, interference, coercion, discrimination, intimidation and retaliation. As
part of the environmental and social assessment process, stakeholder engagement activities are documented, including
the identification of stakeholders consulted, a summary of feedback received, and an explanation of how such feedback
has been considered or the reasons why it was not. In line with ESS10, the Project will enable safe participation and
feedback. This includes providing channels for confidential submissions and, where feasible, anonymous complaints;
communicating that retaliation, intimidation, or coercion associated with engagement or grievance raising is not
acceptable; and ensuring that GRM procedures include safeguards to manage sensitive complaints (including restricted
access to complainant identity and escalation protocols).
The Borrower is required to develop a Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) proportionate to the nature and scale of the
project and its potential risks and impacts . Stakeholders have to be identified and the SEP has to be disclosed for public
review and comment as early as possible, before the project is appraised by the World Bank.
ESS10 also requires the development and implementation of a grievance redress mechanism that allows project-
affected parties and others to raise concerns and provide feedback related to the environmental and social performance
of the project and to have those concerns addressed in a timely manner.
This SEP is an ESS10 instrument focused on information disclosure, consultation, documentation of stakeholder inputs,
and grievance arrangements. Technical assessment and mitigation measures under other Environmental and Social
Standards (e.g., ESS6 biodiversity, ESS5 land acquisition and livelihood restoration, ESS2 labor, ESS4 community health
and safety) are addressed through the ESIA and associated instruments (e.g., CIA, BMP/EFMP, ESMP, LRRF/LRRP, LMP,
ESCP). The SEP ensures that stakeholders can access these instruments, provide comments, and receive documented
responses through the Comment–Response process. The Comment–Response process is publicly documented and
disclosed in accordance with ESS10 requirements. Where stakeholder comments relate to land acquisition, restrictions
on land use, economic displacement, or livelihood impacts, these will be recorded through the Comment–Response
process and the GRM. Substantive ESS5 requirements (eligibility principles, cut-off date approach, compensation at
replacement cost, and livelihood restoration objectives) are established through the Livelihood Restoration and
Resettlement Framework (LRRF) and will be operationalized through site-specific RAPs and/or Livelihood Restoration
Plans (LRPs), as applicable.
Accordingly, the SEP provides the engagement and disclosure mechanism for ESS5 instruments (LRRF/RAP/LRP), but
does not itself define ESS5 entitlements or detailed livelihood restoration measures.
This section summarizes ESS10 requirements. The present SEP documents engagement undertaken to date and defines
arrangements applicable to the current preparation and disclosure stage. Engagement arrangements for subsequent
stages (construction, reservoir impoundment, operation) will be updated and operationalized through future SEP
updates and implementation instruments consistent with the ESCP. Measures to enable safe participation, including
confidentiality and protection from retaliation, are addressed in Chapter 5 and operationalized through the GRM in
Chapter 7.

STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION AND ANALYSIS

3.               SUMMARY OF PREVIOUS STAKEHOLDER ENGAGEMENT
                 ACTIVITIES
Stakeholder engagement activities related to the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Project have been undertaken since 2023,
coinciding with the preparation of the Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA). These activities were
conducted to support information disclosure, consultation, and the collection of stakeholder feedback during the
project preparation stage.
The following main categories of stakeholder engagement activities have been conducted to date:
      1.   Initial meetings under the Kyrgyzstan Renewable Energy Development Project (KRED) in 2023;
      2.   Consultation on Environmental and Social Impact Assessment (ESIA) preparation at local level (October and
           November 2024);
      3.   Consultation on ESIA preparation at national level with governmental, non-governmental, institutions, and
           non-profit organizations (November 2024);
This section provides a factual summary of stakeholder engagement activities already conducted during project
preparation. Detailed records of these consultations, including participant lists, issues raised, and responses provided,
are documented in the Minutes of Meetings (MoM) included as annexes to this SEP. This summary does not introduce
new engagement commitments and is intended to ensure transparency and traceability in line with ESS10
requirements.
All questions and comments raised are captured in the public Comment-Response Matrix (CRM), which provides
traceability from stakeholder inputs to documented responses and references to relevant instruments. This chapter
summarizes what was discussed and clarified during consultations; it does not create new project commitments. Any
commitments are formalized through the ESCP and relevant E&S instruments.

3.1              Key outcomes of past consultation (2023)

Initial stakeholder consultations relevant to the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Project were conducted in January 2023
within the framework of the Kyrgyzstan Renewable Energy Development Project (KRED) Phase 1. The KRED program
covered a broader portfolio of renewable energy investments, including Kambarata-1 HPP, as well as small and medium
hydropower plants and solar power projects. A separate Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) was prepared for the KRED
project in 2023.
As part of the KRED stakeholder engagement process, four (4) consultation workshops were held at the following
locations and dates:
      •    Karakul city on 28th of January 2023;
      •    Sai village on 28th January 2023, Kara-Kulzha rayon of Osh oblast
      •    At the village of Nur, Kemin city dated 30th of January 2023 and
      •    2023 in Bishkek city, 31th January.

documented in the Minutes of Meetings prepared for each consultation.

3.2              Consultation, Participation on ESIA Preparation (2024)
During the preparation of the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Project, structured stakeholder consultations related to the
ESIA process were conducted in October and November 2024. These consultations included one meeting at the national
level and five meetings at the local level.
A subsequent round of consultations was conducted in October and November 2025 to disclose and discuss the
preliminary findings of the environmental and social studies. This round included two national-level meetings, one of
which involved representatives of riparian countries, and five local-level meetings.
Participants in the national-level consultations included representatives of relevant ministries and government
agencies, non-governmental organizations, and other institutional stakeholders. Local-level consultations involved
representatives of local authorities and project-affected persons within the Project Impact Area.
Targeted socio-economic/pasture survey interactions were conducted for baseline data collection and are not counted
as formal public consultations; however, relevant concerns were recorded and fed into ESIA analysis and the CRM where
applicable.

comments.

Statements made during consultations are summarized for transparency; definitive conclusions and commitments are
contained in the disclosed ESIA/CIA and, where applicable, formalized through the ESCP.
The outcomes of the consultation at national level are the followings:
     •    Cumulative impact assessment should be conducted as part of the ESIA study.
     •    Concerns of participants on how the energy distribution will be managed among the countries of Kyrgyzstan,
          Kazakhstan, and Uzbekistan.
     •    Concerns of participants on how the Project will address the potential loss of livelihoods due to the loss of
          pastureland for the population, whose livelihood primarily rely on livestock rearing/breeding.
     •    Apprehension by participants about the emergency situations may arise during the construction and operation
          phase of the project.
     •    There is a view that medical facilities should be developed before construction of the project.
     •    Concerns of participants that project may affect the irrigation sources of the countries sharing water of the
          river.
     •    How will the technical, financial and legal parts of this HPP be regulated among three countries?


The issues summarized above reflect questions and comments raised by participants during the national-level
consultation meeting. These issues have been documented in the consultation records and informed the scope of the
environmental and social assessment.

Note: This summary reflects consultation clarifications and does not constitute a project commitment; any
commitments are formalized through the ESCP and relevant instruments.
These local consultations were conducted as part of the project preparation and environmental and social assessment
process. Their purpose was to inform affected communities about the Project, disclose available preliminary
information, and document concerns and suggestions to be considered in the Environmental and Social Impact
Assessment (ESIA).
The consultations did not constitute decision-making or agreement on compensation, resettlement, infrastructure
provision, employment, or benefit-sharing measures.
Stakeholder engagement activities related to project implementation, construction impacts, compensation
entitlements, livelihood restoration, and community benefit measures will be further defined and operationalized at
subsequent stages of the Project. A dedicated stakeholder engagement methodology will be developed prior to
construction, in coordination with the applicable environmental and social management instruments, to guide ongoing
consultation, information disclosure, and feedback mechanisms during implementation.


The key issues and concerns raised during the local-level consultations are summarized below:
     •    Concerns of local communities that the winter pasturelands used by the villages will be submerged by the
          reservoir, leading to loss of livelihood. There was a request from many regarding construction of bridges and
          new roads to gain access to new winter pastures.
     •    Request from some participants about relocation to other places, preferably near to urban areas.
     •    Concerns of local communities that there may be variation in the climatic conditions of the valleys due to
          formation of a large water body. It was opined that it is not only the submerged land which will be lost but also
          other nearby pasturelands due to change in the climatic conditions of the region.
     •    Many people mentioned that there is dispute between villages for use of pasture lands, especially the winter
          pastures. They were apprehensive that reduction in pasture lands due to the submergence with intensify the
          disputes unless pasture lands are not properly demarcated.
     •    -Participants expressed concerns regarding entitlement to compensation for the loss of residential/ non-
          residential structure and/ or pasture land.
All concerns raised during the local consultations have been documented and will be considered as part of the ESIA
analysis. Proposed mitigation and management measures will be subject to further assessment, disclosure, and
consultation at subsequent stages of the Project. These issues will be further discussed, along with proposed mitigation
measures, during the next round of stakeholder consultations planned for Q4 2025.
The consultations summarized above focused on disclosure and collection of feedback. They did not involve negotiation
or agreement on compensation rates, land allocation, relocation sites, or benefit-sharing arrangements.

3.3              Consultation, Participation on ESIA Results (2025)
3.3.1            2nd round Consultation at National Level on ESIA
The MoE organized national consultations in Bishkek, on 15 October 2025 regarding the Ecological and Social Study
Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant.
Following the disclosure of the draft final environmental and social documents, the purpose of the consultation was to
let all interested parties (governmental, non-governmental, institutions, and non-profit organizations) ask questions,
express their concerns, and provide suggestions on the results of the studies.
A total of 51 participants took part in the consultations. Participants also included local and international NGOs, various
Institutional groups, from local media, research educational bodies such as Association of Women in Energy,
environmental coalition, district level regulatory bodies (Toktogul district akim), various ministries such as National
Institute of Public Health of the Ministry of Health, Ministry of Emergency Situations and different individual from
epidemic background such as political scientist etc.
Online participation was also organized for those interested stakeholders. The link to the event was provided in advance.
All necessary efforts were made to ensure that the consultations were inclusive, taking into account the diverse interests
and needs.
The Consultation included all project-related aspects, including environmental and social, legal, technical,
compensation, management, and communication matters. Suggested proposals and feedback from participants were
taken into consideration. The main concerns raised during the meetings and the answers from the Project team are
summarized in the table below. Minutes of each meetings are presented in Annex 3.
Based on the presentation and information provided by MoE, questions and issues were raised by the participants. The
summary of the issues raised during the consultation is given below. The minutes of meetings are presented in Annex
A2.1.

and publication of consultation materials through official channels to enable participation beyond intergovernmental
settings. Records of such engagement (participant lists, issues raised, and responses) will be documented in meeting
minutes and reflected in the Response Matrix.

3.4              Next Steps of ESIA Consultations
3.4.1            National Level Consultations
The next steps regarding the national level consultation will be as follows:
      1.   Information disclosure
Providing timely and necessary background information of the Project to participants are paramount. It will make sure
the participant receives easy-to-understand information, and asked them to be prepared to provide feedback during
the consultation. Sharing information and receiving feedback graciously is an integral part of communications between
policy- or decision makers and their constituents.
After the consultation, it was always good practice to share the information back with the participants. This information
sharing will lead to inform discussions which will help to improve project and strengthen relationships between
policymakers and people. Timely and accessible disclosure of relevant project information will continue to be
undertaken in accordance with applicable national requirements and World Bank ESS10. Information will be presented
in a manner that is understandable and appropriate to the audience, to support informed feedback during
consultations.


Following consultations, summary information will be shared, as appropriate, to reflect key issues raised and next
procedural steps. Further information disclosure arrangements related to project implementation will be defined and
operationalized at subsequent stages of the Project.
      2.   Close the feedback loop


Closing the feedback loop is an important element of meaningful consultation. Where appropriate, participants will be
informed of how issues raised during consultations have been documented and how they will be considered within the
ESIA process or subsequent project planning stages.
This approach supports transparency and helps manage stakeholder expectations regarding the scope and limitations
of consultation outcomes.
      3.   Staff Instructions and Awareness-Raising Materials
The Access to Information will set the step-by-step procedures to guide the Project staff in making operational
information and documents publicly available. The staff instructions will list all documents and titles that are publicly
available. Separate awareness-raising materials will be developed and updated as needed to guide EA in implementing
the policy and to inform other interested stakeholders on the policy provisions. Procedures for access to information
will guide Project staff in making relevant project information publicly available in accordance with national legislation

4.               STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION AND ANALYSIS
4.1              Categories of Stakeholders
ESS10 identifies two broad categories of stakeholders: "Project Affected Parties" and "Other Interested Parties". ESS10
also requires that the Borrower will identify those project-affected parties (individuals or groups) who, due to their
particular circumstances, may be disadvantaged or vulnerable.
Project affected parties (PAPs) are individuals, groups and other entities in the project area who are directly or
indirectly, positively or negatively (actually or potentially) affected by the project and/or who have been identified as
the most susceptible to project-related changes. These stakeholders should be actively involved in identifying impacts
and their significance, as well as in providing input to the development of mitigation and management measures.
Other interested parties are individuals, groups, or organizations who may not be directly affected by the Project but
who have an interest in the Project or the ability to influence its outcomes or implementation process. In the case of
the Project, this will include non-governmental and civil society organizations, including those operating at regional or
transboundary level, which may have an interest in cumulative environmental and social impacts, information
disclosure, and public participation related to the Project
Vulnerable groups are individuals or groups who may be disproportionately affected or further disadvantaged by the
Project compared to others due to their specific circumstances or characteristics. Special measures may be required to
ensure their meaningful participation and equal representation in stakeholder engagement processes.

4.2              Stakeholders Identification and Prioritization
Stakeholder identification aims to identify and categorize stakeholders relevant to the Kambarata-1 Hydropower Project
(K1-HPP) for stakeholder engagement purposes. Stakeholder identification is an ongoing process, and additional
stakeholders may be identified at different stages of project development.
For the purpose of stakeholder prioritization, the level of interest and influence has been assessed qualitatively based
on the following criteria:
     (i)    degree of potential exposure to environmental and social impacts;
     (ii)   dependence on project-affected resources (e.g. land, pastures, water);
   (iii)    legal or institutional mandate relevant to project approval, oversight, or implementation;
   (iv)     capacity to influence project decisions, public opinion, or implementation outcomes; and
     (v)    relevance to cumulative or transboundary considerations.
This prioritization is indicative and intended to support proportionate engagement planning in line with ESS10. It does
not imply decision-making authority or entitlement to project benefits.
Stakeholders have been categorized based on their level of interest in the Project and their relative influence on project
outcomes. For the purposes of this SEP, stakeholders have been classified into three categories: (i) high; (ii) medium;
and (iii) low interest and influence. This categorization is intended to support a proportionate and risk-informed
approach to stakeholder engagement, in line with ESS10, by considering stakeholders’ potential exposure to project
impacts and their ability to influence or be affected by project decisions.

STAKEHOLDER IDENTIFICATION AND ANALYSIS

Stakeholder identification and prioritization is an iterative process. As the Project progresses and additional information
becomes available, new stakeholders or stakeholder groups may be identified and included in the engagement process.
The SEP will be updated accordingly to reflect such changes.
The identified stakeholder groups and their categorization are presented in Table 4.1.

4.3              Vulnerable groups

Disadvantaged or vulnerable individuals and groups may be disproportionately affected, either adversely or beneficially,
by the Project. Due to socio-economic factors, their views may not be adequately represented in formal decision-making
processes, or their concerns may not be fully captured through standard consultation approaches.
Identification of vulnerable individuals and groups is an important step in ensuring their meaningful engagement during
the ESIA process. This enables the Project implementing agency (K1-PMO) to consider measures aimed at avoiding,
minimizing, or mitigating potential adverse impacts on vulnerable groups, and to inform the design of appropriate
management frameworks where relevant.
Early consideration of vulnerable groups during project preparation can also support the identification of opportunities
to enhance inclusiveness and mitigate risks related to livelihood disruption, in a manner consistent with the Project’s
scope and stage of development.
Vulnerable groups under the project may include the following categories, among others:
      1) Low-income households and individuals;
      2) The elderly, elderly persons ;
      3) People with disabilities; and
      4) Women headed low-income households.
In the context of the Kambarata-1 HPP, vulnerability may also arise from livelihood dependence on seasonal or
common-use natural resources, including pastoralists and households relying on transhumant grazing practices, even
where such households may not fall below formal income thresholds.
Vulnerability will therefore be assessed using both socio-economic criteria and context-specific factors, including
livelihood dependency, seasonality of land use, remoteness, and limited access to information or services.
It is noted that the above categories have been identified based on the current stage of the project. These categories
of vulnerable households will be reconfirmed during the socio-economic survey (SES) conducted as part of the
Resettlement Plans (RPs) preparation, and the list will be updated accordingly as the project progresses.
Vulnerability may also be contextual and may arise due to limited access to information, geographic remoteness,
language barriers, or reduced capacity to participate effectively in consultation processes.

The influence attributed to vulnerable groups reflects their limited direct capacity to affect project decision-making, while recognizing that their concerns carry heightened
significance due to their potential exposure to adverse impacts. ESS10 requires that such groups receive targeted and inclusive engagement, irrespective of their formal level of
influence.

STAKEHOLDER ENGAGEMENT PROGRAM




5.               STAKEHOLDER ENGAGEMENT PROGRAM
5.1              Planned Stakeholder Engagement Activities
Stakeholder engagement activities need to provide specific stakeholder groups with relevant information and
opportunities to voice their views on topics that matter to them. The Table 55-1 below presents the stakeholder
engagement activities envisaged under the project. The activity types and their frequency are adapted to the three main
project stages (preparation, implementation and project design; construction; post-construction and operation phase).
The Stakeholder Engagement Plan (SEP) outlines the engagement process, methods, including sequencing, topics of
consultations and target stakeholders. The SEP operationalizes safe participation through confidential channels and the
GRM as described in Sections 5.2.7 and Chapter 7.
The project will mostly employ interviews, focus group discussions, open meetings, and workshops as a means of
carrying out consultations. This will be done right in the 10 affected villages to enable easy participation of vulnerable
and disadvantaged groups of people. The strategy will also be different depending on the Project stages as presented
below.
      1.   Project preparation stage (before project appraisal). While some technical details of the K1-HPP will be
           unknown at this stage, the consultation will focus on a broader range of stakeholders to understand their
           general interests and concerns on the project and its potential positive and negative impacts to inform the
           project’s environmental and social frameworks. This is also the stage where potential
           vulnerable/disadvantaged groups relevant to the project will be identified.
      2.   Pre-construction, dam construction stage. At this stage, the technical details of the K1-HPP will be determined
           and specific project-affected parties (PAPs) identified. The consultation will focus on PAPs to address their
           concerns through the implementation of subproject-specific environmental and social plans. . The Project will
           maintain project-level channels for receiving and addressing concerns, including through the GRM, as further
           detailed prior to construction. K1-PMO will apply inclusive engagement measures for vulnerable groups,
           consistent with ESS10.
      3.   Reservoir impoundment. At this stage, the consultation will focus on the PAPs to address their concerns
           regarding the livelihood restoration program implementation and the restoration of access and the dam safety
           and community health and safety. The project will ensure that the GRM remain available during reservoir
           impoundment to promptly address any grievances from the PAPs. The K1-PMO will pay particular attention to
           involve vulnerable groups in the income restoration program and in the community health and safety.
      4.   Post construction/ maintenance stage. At this stage, the consultation will focus on the feedback from PAPs on
           the performance of environmental and social mitigations conducted during the construction and reservoir
           impoundment stages and in particular on the livelihood restoration program. The consultation on employment
           opportunities for O&M works will be also conducted. Areas for improvement and lessons learned should be
           identified for the following subprojects. Dissemination and reporting back to general stakeholders on the
           outcome of the monitoring and evaluation of the project will be also arranged.
For clarity, this SEP focuses on stakeholder engagement undertaken at the current stage of project preparation and ESIA
disclosure. Engagement activities described for later stages are indicative and will be further detailed and
operationalized prior to implementation, in line with ESS10.
Accountability for stakeholder engagement under ESS10 rests with the Borrower, represented by the Ministry of Energy
and the K1-PMO. Consultants and contractors may support implementation; however, they do not replace the
Borrower’s responsibility for ensuring meaningful engagement, proper documentation, and functioning grievance
arrangements. Roles of contractors in community engagement will be defined in contractual requirements and will be
supervised by K1-PMO.

RESOURCES AND RESPONSIBILITIES FOR IMPLEMENTING STAKEHOLDER ENGAGEMENT ACTIVITIES




5.1.1.1          Engagement on Transboundary and Cumulative Impact Topics
Engagement related to cumulative and transboundary environmental and social impacts will focus on information
disclosure, documentation of concerns, and feedback mechanisms. Technical design and implementation of mitigation
measures will be addressed through relevant project instruments.

5.2              Methods to Be Used
5.2.1            Public consultations / hearings


At the commencement of project implementation, it is envisaged that project launch meetings will be held at national
level and in affected Aiyl Aimaks and villages, subject to finalization of institutional arrangements. These meetings are
intended to provide information on the Project and the next steps of implementation.
During construction and reservoir impoundment, public consultations and focused discussions with project-affected
parties are expected to be undertaken on a periodic basis to provide opportunities for stakeholders, including vulnerable
groups, to receive information and express their views on key project-related activities. Specific consultation formats,
frequency, and modalities will be further defined and operationalized at later stages of the Project.
Meetings are expected to follow an open-house format, allowing information to be presented by the Project
implementing entity and enabling participants to raise questions and concerns. A range of consultation approaches may
be applied for different stakeholder groups, including separate meetings with women and vulnerable households where
appropriate, to better understand their needs, expectations, and concerns in relation to the Project.
Feedback received through consultations will be documented and considered in the ongoing refinement of
environmental and social management instruments, in accordance with ESS10.

5.2.2            Training, workshops


Capacity-building activities on selected social issues may be undertaken for K1-PMO staff and, where relevant, other
government or non-government service providers, subject to project needs and institutional arrangements. Indicative
topics may include awareness-raising on social inclusion and exclusion, labor-related issues, and risks related to gender-
based violence.
The scope, timing, and modalities of such training and workshop activities will be further defined at later stages of the
Project.

5.2.3            Grievance redress mechanism


In accordance with the World Bank’s Environmental and Social Standard 10 (ESS10) and relevant national practices in
the Kyrgyz Republic, the Project will establish a project-level grievance redress mechanism to receive and address
concerns, complaints, and feedback related to environmental and social aspects of the Project. The grievance
mechanism is intended to be aligned with, and complementary to, existing national and local administrative grievance
channels available to citizens.
Appropriate communication materials are expected to be prepared to support stakeholder awareness of grievance
submission channels and procedures. The Project implementing entity will coordinate with relevant administrative
authorities to support the recording and tracking of grievances in a manner proportionate to the Project stage and
institutional arrangements.
Initial efforts to resolve grievances are expected to take place at the lowest appropriate administrative level, with
escalation procedures defined as needed. Grievances may be submitted anonymously; however, in such cases,
confirmation of complainant satisfaction may not be possible. Sensitive grievances will be handled through a
confidential pathway with restricted access, consistent with safe participation principles.




Kambarata-1 HPP                                                  Official Use Only   Client Reference No. 5018007
P21: Stakeholder Engagement Plan & Grievance Redress Mechanism                       SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
                                                                                     25 February 2026                        57
RESOURCES AND RESPONSIBILITIES FOR IMPLEMENTING STAKEHOLDER ENGAGEMENT ACTIVITIES



Information on the grievance mechanism, including available submission channels and general procedural steps, will be
disclosed through project information materials and relevant websites. Detailed grievance handling procedures, roles,
and timelines are described in Chapter 7 and will be further refined at later stages of the Project, as appropriate.

5.2.4            Mass/social media communication
Mass media and social media may be used as supplementary channels for information disclosure and stakeholder
outreach, in addition to other engagement methods described in this SEP. Project-related information may be
disseminated through official Ministry of Energy or Project communication platforms, as appropriate.
The use of social media channels may support broader outreach and timely information sharing, taking into account
prevailing communication practices and accessibility among different stakeholder groups in the project-affected areas.
The specific platforms, content, and frequency of communication will be determined in line with project needs and
institutional arrangements at later stages of the Project.
Where messaging applications (e.g., WhatsApp/Telegram) are used for project communications, participation will be
voluntary and based on informed consent. Group administration will follow basic data protection principles, including:
limiting collection of personal data to what is necessary; not disclosing participants’ personal contact details beyond the
group context; providing options to submit questions privately; and moderating content to prevent intimidation or
retaliation. These measures will be applied consistent with national personal data protection requirements.

5.2.5            Information Desks
Information desks may be established in project-affected villages as part of stakeholder engagement and information
disclosure activities. Where established, such desks may provide local communities with accessible information on the
Project, stakeholder engagement arrangements, available communication channels, and contact details of relevant
Project representatives.
Information boards may be displayed at village level in locations accessible to project-affected persons, providing
general project information and guidance on the grievance redress mechanism. Brochures and flyers covering relevant
environmental and social topics may also be made available through these channels.
Written project information may be disclosed through a range of communication materials, including leaflets and
posters. In addition, key project-related information and updates may be disclosed through official Ministry of Energy
or Project websites, as appropriate, in Russian and Kyrgyz languages. Information on the grievance redress mechanism
will also be made publicly available through these channels.
The specific locations, formats, content, and frequency of information disclosure will be determined and further detailed
at subsequent stages of the Project, in line with institutional arrangements and implementation needs.

5.2.6            Citizen/PAP perception survey
A perception survey examining citizen’s experience and feedback about the project is expected to be undertaken at
least twice, subject to project needs and implementation arrangements.

5.2.7            Protection Against Retaliation and Safe Participation
The Project recognizes the importance of enabling stakeholders to express views freely and without fear of retaliation.
The SEP provides for confidential engagement channels, anonymous grievance submission, and non-disclosure of
personal information where requested. Participation in stakeholder engagement activities is voluntary and does not
affect access to services, compensation, or employment opportunities.

5.3              Strategy to incorporate views of vulnerable groups

Vulnerable groups may be consulted using tailored and appropriate engagement methods, as necessary. The methods
of interaction envisaged under the Project are described in the sections below.
Project information, including selected environmental and social documents (such as the SEP, ESIA and RPs), project
updates, and information on the grievance mechanism, may be disclosed through the official website and social media
channels of the Ministry of Energy in Russian and Kyrgyz languages. Where access to the Internet is limited or not

feasible, alternative and traditional methods—such as face-to-face meetings, focus group discussions, information
stands, and distribution of printed materials—may be used to inform and engage vulnerable groups.
Further consultations aimed at refining the identification of vulnerable groups, based on updated social assessment
information, may be undertaken at subsequent stages of the Project, in line with the evolution of project design and
institutional arrangements.
As indicated in this SEP, the Project intends to apply special measures to facilitate equitable participation of vulnerable
social groups, including access to information, opportunities to provide feedback, and use of the grievance mechanism.
The following indicative mechanisms may be used to support information disclosure and engagement with vulnerable
groups:
   (i)    Social networks and traditional media for any announcements related to the project. These include the official
          web pages of the ministries, as well as social media (Facebook, Instagram, Telegram) and official newspapers.
   (ii)   Publication of all information about the project, including the GM process, in Russian/Kyrgyz;
  (iii)   In the case of public meetings, including workshops or meetings, these meetings should be held at a suitable
          time and place for women, and should also take into account the needs of persons with disabilities (if any), or
          the barriers faced by vulnerable population groups. These meetings are often more conveniently held in public
          spaces or in women's civil society organizations (if available).
