5.4              Information Disclosure

Information disclosure under this SEP is intended to be carried out through the Ministry of Energy website and other
appropriate communication channels, in a manner proportionate to the nature and stage of the Project. Information
made publicly available may include stakeholder mapping, a summary of the SEP, and indicative stakeholder
engagement activities. Copies of disclosed documents will be maintained by the K1-PMO for reference purposes.
Following the disclosure of the draft SEP on the K1-PMO or Ministry of Energy website, information on the availability
of the document may be disseminated through appropriate public channels, including newspaper announcements, to
inform stakeholders and invite comments, as relevant. An executive summary of the SEP, translated into Russian and
Kyrgyz languages, may be distributed to key stakeholders. Information brochures and leaflets related to stakeholder
engagement and grievance mechanisms may also be made available through the Project website.
The final SEP, incorporating comments received where applicable, will be submitted to the World Bank for review and
subsequent disclosure in accordance with the Bank’s requirements.

5.5              Review of Comments

Comments received through the stakeholder engagement process, including written and oral inputs, will be
documented and reviewed by the K1-PMO environmental and social (E&S) team. Contact details for the K1-PMO E&S
team will be disclosed to stakeholders during consultation meetings. Minutes of consultations will be supplemented,
where applicable, with written comments received within the agreed timeframe following each consultation.
Stakeholder comments will be consolidated in a comment–response format, providing an official response to the issues
raised. This summary may be shared with stakeholders through appropriate channels, including public disclosure via the
Ministry of Energy website, as relevant. Consultation minutes and response summaries are expected to be made
available in English, Russian, and Kyrgyz, where feasible.


Kambarata-1 HPP                                                  Official Use Only    Client Reference No. 5018007
P21: Stakeholder Engagement Plan & Grievance Redress Mechanism                        SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
                                                                                      25 February 2026                           60
RESOURCES AND RESPONSIBILITIES FOR IMPLEMENTING STAKEHOLDER ENGAGEMENT ACTIVITIES



The SEP will remain publicly available throughout project preparation and implementation and may be updated
periodically to reflect project developments, stakeholder feedback, or changes in project design or institutional
arrangements. Stakeholder engagement methods will be reviewed from time to time to assess their effectiveness and
relevance and adjusted as necessary to maintain meaningful engagement in line with ESS10.
Stakeholder comments received on the ESIA package, including from civil society organizations, have been documented
in a consolidated Comment–Response Matrix (A-2-3). The Matrix summarizes each issue raised and provides the
Borrower’s response, including references to the relevant E&S instruments where the issue will be addressed (e.g., ESIA,
CIA, EFMP, BMP, ESCP, and this SEP).
The Comment–Response Matrix will be disclosed together with the updated SEP, and meeting minutes and disclosure
logs will be made available as appendices to support traceability and transparency. Where comments relate to technical
mitigation measures under ESS6, the SEP ensures that these issues are routed to the appropriate instruments and that
stakeholders are informed of how the feedback has been considered.
This SEP update reflects responses to stakeholder comments related to engagement processes under ESS10 and does
not modify or pre-empt commitments under other Environmental and Social Standards.

6.                  RESOURCES AND RESPONSIBILITIES
6.1                 Resources
For the implementation of the Kambarata-1 HPP, the Ministry of Energy of the Kyrgyz Republic has established a Project
Management Office (K1-PMO). The K1-PMO is located in Bishkek and is headed by a Project Manager. Stakeholder
engagement activities at community level will mostly be done by K1-PMO E&S team who will be supported by the
relevant departments at village level. Overall management responsibility for implementing the SEP will rest with the K1-
PMO Environmental, Health, Safety, Security and Social (EHSS) Manager and Social Development Specialist. The EHSS
Manager has overall responsibility for coordination and oversight of environmental and social safeguard instruments,
including the SEP.
The Social Development Specialist is supported by a Stakeholder Liaison Officer and Community Liaison Officers at the
Ayil Aimak level, who facilitate community outreach and consultations, support the operation of the grievance redress
mechanism, and assist in documenting stakeholder engagement activities and reported concerns. Other specialists and
officers within the K1-PMO contribute, as relevant, to stakeholder engagement activities in line with their respective
functional responsibilities.
The K1-PMO is responsible for coordinating and overseeing the implementation of the Project SEP, in accordance with
the World Bank ESS10 and national requirements. Its main tasks are:
(i)            Preparation of the SEP to submit to the MoE for approval;
(ii)    Preparation of periodic environmental and social performance reports to the World Bank in accordance with
the agreed reporting arrangements (including ESCP requirements).
Regarding GRM implementation, the K1-PMO will be assisted by other relevant agencies depending on the category of
grievance as described in Chapter 7. A focal person will be identified within the K1-PMO to answer to questions
regarding the GRM. The contact information of the K1-PMO and other key persons is as follows:

addressing their grievances.

7.               GRIEVANCE REDRESS MECHANISM
7.1              General Provisions
This Grievance Redress Mechanism (GRM) is created as a Project mechanism at the local and national levels for the
receipt, registration, consideration, assessment, resolution of grievances and decision-making on them arising in the
process of implementing the K1-HPP Project, in accordance with the national legislation of the Kyrgyz Republic and the
World Bank’s Environmental and Social Framework (ESF).
This GRM applies to project-affected persons and other external stakeholders. Grievances from Project workers,
including contractor and subcontractor personnel, will be addressed through a separate worker grievance mechanism
in accordance with ESS2 (Labor and Working Conditions).
Appeals in the form of obtaining information, recommendations, requests from citizens for assistance in the
implementation and restoration of their constitutional rights and freedoms or constitutional rights and freedoms of
other persons, or a message about the violation of laws and other regulatory legal acts, as well as about shortcomings
in the activities of state bodies and local government bodies and their officials not related to the implementation of
administrative procedures, are considered taking into account the Law of the Kyrgyz Republic “On the procedure for
considering citizens' appeals” dated May 4, 2007 No. 67 and the WB ESS10 “Stakeholder Engagement and Information
Disclosure” requirements.
Grievances directed at the actions or inactions of state and municipal bodies that have resulted in legal and /or actual
consequences for individuals or legal entities as a result of the implementation of the Project shall be considered taking
into account the Law of the Kyrgyz Republic “On the Fundamentals of Administrative Activity and Administrative
Procedure” (dated July 31, 2015, No. 210) and the WB ESS10 “Stakeholder Engagement and Information Disclosure”
requirements.
A detailed GRM will be further developed and approved by the Ministry of Energy prior to Project implementation,
consistent with the principles and framework outlined in this SEP and in accordance with applicable national legislation
and World Bank ESS requirements. This section establishes the overarching principles, scope, and procedural
framework for the Project Grievance Redress Mechanism. Detailed operational procedures, institutional arrangements,
and implementation tools will be finalized and formalized prior to commencement of Project implementation, in line
with this framework and applicable national legislation. The GRM is designed to allow confidential and anonymous
submission of grievances, including concerns related to stakeholder engagement processes, without risk of retaliation.
Grievances related to land acquisition, land use restrictions and involuntary resettlement, livelihood impacts, unfair
compensation, inadequacy of rights, inaccuracies in detailed measurements, or problems with the donation process,
etc., will be addressed taking into account the GRM details given in the LRRF.
For grievances related to land acquisition, involuntary resettlement, compensation eligibility, valuation, or livelihood
restoration measures, the detailed grievance procedures defined under the LRRF and subsequent LRRPs prevail.
This SEP GRM provides the entry point, tracking, and referral framework, without altering ESS5-specific rights,
entitlements, or procedures.

7.2              Principles
This GRM is based on following fundamental principles for handling grievances and guarantees:
      •   Availability: GRM should be easily accessible to all external interested parties, taking into account
          confidentiality, legal obligations and vulnerable persons and groups.
      •   Efficiency and effectiveness: grievances shall be handled effectively, promptly, objectively, sensitively,
          responsibly and transparently
      •   Communication: Provide applicants with timely and continuous information on the progress of the review
          grievances, including reasons for any delays.
      •   Impartiality: Those responsible for handling grievances shall act impartially and shall have no basis for favoring
          one party or outcome over another.


Kambarata-1 HPP                                                  Official Use Only   Client Reference No. 5018007
P21: Stakeholder Engagement Plan & Grievance Redress Mechanism                       SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
                                                                                     25 February 2026                          67
MONITORING AND REPORTING

      •    No cost: access to the GRM and grievance resolution shall be free of charge to complainants.
      •   No restrictions on civil or legal rights: the mandatory consideration of anonymous grievances, participation in
          the GRM should not hinder or complicate the victim's right to other judicial, administrative, civil or other means
          of legal protection not prohibited by the legislation of the Kyrgyz Republic.
      •   Confidentiality and anonymity: All grievances shall be handled confidentially. Anonymous grievances may be
          submitted and will be considered to the extent feasible, without limiting the complainant’s access to other
          administrative or judicial remedies.
      •   The fundamental rights and interests of PAPs must be considered and protected with special attention;
      •   Where appropriate, culturally appropriate and customary dispute resolution practices may be considered as
          complementary mechanisms, provided they are consistent with national legislation, ESS requirements, and the
          principles of fairness, inclusiveness and non-discrimination.

7.3              GRM procedure
The GRM shall include the following:
      •   Various ways for applicants to file grievances, including filing grievances in person or through a representative,
          by telephone, by text message, by mail, by email or through a website;
      •   Maintaining a written and electronic record of all grievances in a centralized grievance log.
      •   Publicly disclosed procedures defining indicative timeframes for grievance acknowledgement, assessment,
          response and resolution. As a general reference, grievances will be acknowledged within a short period
          following receipt, assessed promptly, and resolved within indicative timeframes defined in this SEP, subject to
          the nature and complexity of the grievance.
      •   An escalation and appeals process, including access to relevant administrative and judicial remedies under the
          national legal framework, without prejudice to the complainant’s right to pursue legal action at any stage.

7.3.1            GRM Levels
The grievance redress mechanism is an integral part of the institutional arrangement in relation to the environmental
and social risk management. A two-stage process will be established for the Project as described below. Table 7-1and
Figure 7-1 summarize this process. The indicative timeframes may vary depending on the nature and complexity of the
grievance and the need for additional verification.
The complainant will be provided with assistance in relation to the grievances handling process at all levels of
consideration. Complainants have the right to ask any questions, express concerns or complain to local government
officials, state authorities or Project staff.
First level, Local. At the village level (Aiyl Okmotu) local contact persons receive/register grievances from applicants.
Complaint Registration Log Form) and inform the K1-PMO. After consideration of grievances with involvement of the
K1-PMO relevant staff and local self-government authorities, local contact persons, in coordination with the K1-PMO
and relevant local self-government authorities, provide a response to the applicant within 14 working days.If the
Applicant is not satisfied and/or the issue cannot be resolved at the first level of the GRM, the complaint is escalated to
the second (central) level.
Second level, Central. At the second level, the K1-PMO responsible staff receives and registers the complaint and
forwards it to the K1PMO director. The K1-PMO director assigns the PMO staff responsible for resolution of the
complaint, further together with other relevant staff, they will review the grievance and determine appropriate
resolution measures. Indicative response timelines are aligned with national legislation and are applied in a
proportionate manner, depending on the nature and complexity of the grievance. Complaints requiring a collegial
decision are reviewed by a GRM committee established by the MoE.
For grievances in the form of obtaining information, recommendations, requests from citizens not related to the
implementation of administrative procedures, a decision is made within 14 working days, and for grievances directed
at the actions or inactions of state and municipal bodies that have entailed legal and /or factual consequences for
individuals or legal entities, a decision is made within 30 days.

P21: Stakeholder Engagement Plan & Grievance Redress Mechanism                       SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
                                                                                     25 February 2026                          68
MONITORING AND REPORTING

If special checks, additional materials or other measures are required to resolve a complaint, the resolution period may
be extended: it can be prolonged exceptionally for no longer than 30 calendar days for the complaints not related to
the implementation of administrative procedures and no longer 60 calendar days for the complaints directed at the
actions or inactions of state and municipal bodies that have entailed legal and /or factual consequences for individuals
or legal entities.
The GRM should not interfere with or hinder the applicant’s right to other legal remedies. The applicant may terminate
participation in the project GRM process at any time and seek resolution through the courts in accordance with the
legislation of the Kyrgyz Republic.

7.3.4            Grievance recording and database
All received grievances, including requests, proposals, complaints, are subject to registration in the Grievances Log
which is maintained both in electronic and paper format. The LFPs at local level and the K1-PMO focal point at central
level register received grievances, providing detailed information such as the date of registration, applicant's
information and contact details, the essence and reasons for the appeal, and the category of the grievance.
All the above-mentioned LFPs at local level and the PMO responsible staff (for GRM registration, coordination and
monitoring) at central level have access to the Grievance Log to register and monitor the timeframes for reviewing
grievances. If necessary, they may record justified extensions of the grievance review timeframe, provided there is a
justification for the extension and and inform the complainant in writing in case of any justified extension..

7.4              Handling SEA/SH grievances
All World Bank-financed projects must comply with the WB ESF requirements on the prevention of Sexual Exploitation
and Abuse/Sexual Harassment (SEA/SH) and implement measures to raise awareness and mitigate the risks of SEA/SH.
The handling of SEA/SH grievances will follow a survivor-centered approach, prioritizing the safety, confidentiality,
dignity, and informed consent of survivors. No action will be taken without the survivor’s consent, and reporting will
not require the provision of personal identifying information.
The Project will develop a clear and transparent GRM process for receiving, investigating, and resolving SEA/SH
grievances. The GRM will ensure access and confidentiality for the consideration of special grievances, allowing
complainants to voice their concerns without fear of retaliation. These grievances will be handled promptly through
confidential and survivor-centered procedures, with referral to appropriate services and mechanisms as required.
The K1-PMO will designate SEA/SH focal points within the PMO and require contractors to identify trained personnel
responsible for SEA/SH-related matters.
The Project will use additional mitigation measures proportionate to the risk:
      •   Establish a secure and confidential reporting system for SEA/SH grievances, accessible to all community
          members.
      •   Conduct regular training sessions for project staff, contractors, and community members on SEA/SH
          prevention, reporting procedures, and the importance of confidentiality.
      •   Disseminate information about SEA/SH risks, prevention measures, and available support services through
          community meetings, posters, and leaflets.
      •   Ensure that all data related to SEA/SH grievances are securely stored and only accessible to authorized
          personnel.
      •   Establish clear procedures for confidential intake, referral, and follow-up of SEA/SH grievances.
      •   Establish partnerships with local organizations and service providers to offer comprehensive support to
          survivors.
      •   Regularly monitor the implementation of SEA/SH prevention measures and the effectiveness of the GRM.
      •   Include aggregated and non-identifiable information on SEA/SH-related awareness activities and GRM
          functionality in project progress reports, without disclosure of personal or case-specific details.



Kambarata-1 HPP                                                  Official Use Only   Client Reference No. 5018007
P21: Stakeholder Engagement Plan & Grievance Redress Mechanism                       SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
                                                                                     25 February 2026                      72
MONITORING AND REPORTING

The Project GRM does not investigate SEA/SH cases. Its role is limited to confidential intake, referral, and monitoring of
access to services, in accordance with survivor consent.



7.5              OPERATIONAL PROTOCOL: SEA/SH GRIEVANCE REDRESS MECHANISM
To support operationalizing the SEA/SH GRM a step-by-step, survivor-centred protocol is proposed to clearly outline:

      1.   Who handles SEA/SH cases,

      2.   How cases enter the system (and how anonymity works),

      3.   What happens from intake → response → referral → closure,

      4.   What must not happen (no mediation, no questioning survivors, no community committees),

      5.   Special timelines,

      6.   Confidential documentation rules,

      7.   Required training and survivor-support pathways.

7.5.1            Purpose
This protocol will help to establishes clear, confidential, survivor-centred procedures for receiving, documenting,
managing, referring, and resolving SEA/SH grievances under the Project. It operationalizes the SEP’s commitment to
safe reporting, confidentiality, specialised handling, and non-retaliation.

The key aspects of this protocol are summarised as:

7.5.1.1          Dedicated Reporting Channels that are always Confidential & Secure
To achieve confidentiality and security, SEA/SH complaints must always bypass the general GRM channels and be
received only by trained SEA/SH focal points.

Confidential channels can include the following:

      •    SEA/SH Hotline (24/7) – answered only by trained focal points and/or secure SMS/WhatsApp for confidential
           messages or voice notes.

      •    Direct reporting to K1-PMO SEA/SH Focal Point or Contractor SEA/SH Officer.

      •    Anonymous submission through online form/QR code or sealed drop-box.

      •    Referral from trusted intermediaries (women’s groups, NGOs, health workers).

Local government or village committee staff must not receive, screen, or investigate SEA/SH cases.

Response must be immediate Response (within 24 hours) and upon receiving a complaint—anonymous or identified—
the SEA/SH focal point shall:

1.    Acknowledge receipt within 24 hours (only if safe and not anonymous).

2.    Prioritize survivor safety, including safety planning if needed.

3.    Offer immediate access to:

      •    medical care



Kambarata-1 HPP                                                  Official Use Only   Client Reference No. 5018007
P21: Stakeholder Engagement Plan & Grievance Redress Mechanism                       SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
                                                                                     25 February 2026                        73
MONITORING AND REPORTING

     •    psychosocial first aid

     •    safe shelter (if relevant)

     •    legal support / police referral (only with survivor consent)

All actions must follow a survivor-centred approach: respect, confidentiality, non-judgment, and no pressure to pursue
any option.

7.5.1.2          Always Follow Case Handling Procedures
a) Intake & Documentation must cover only essential information, using a coded case ID, not names. It is recommended
that a separate, encrypted SEA/SH log accessible only to: K1-PMO SEA/SH Focal Point and PMC Social/SEA Specialist.
No physical copies stored in community offices.
b) Investigation Pathways include:

1.   If the alleged perpetrator is a project worker:

          •     Contractor SEA/SH Officer is notified immediately.

          •     Worker is removed from victim contact areas pending investigation.

          •     Contractor initiates disciplinary procedures aligned with the Code of Conduct.

2.   If the alleged perpetrator is not linked to the project:

          •     Offer referral to police or legal services only with survivor consent.

It will never be permissible to allow community mediation, questioning the survivor, confronting the alleged
perpetrator, group investigations by village bodies (as these violate confidentiality obligations).
Timelines for SEA/SH cases must follow special timeframes distinct from the general 14–30 day GRM timelines.

 Action                                                               Timeline
 Survivor contacted (if safe)                                         ≤ 24 hrs
 Connection to services                                               ≤ 48 hrs
 Case review by SEA/SH focal point                                    ≤ 72 hrs
 Investigation/contractor disciplinary process                        ≤ 10 days
 Closure (survivor-led)                                               Flexible


7.5.1.3          Referral Pathways are well documented
To enable SEA/SH focal points maintain a confidential and up to date directory of service providers, including: medical
facilities offering clinical care, psychosocial support services, legal aid providers, police GBV units, shelters (if available).
Referral is only with explicit survivor consent, except when required by law for children.

7.5.1.4          Feedback to Survivor will depend on their preferences.
Survivors may choose to receive updates at intervals they choose, opt out of follow-up, or remain fully anonymous. A
case is closed only with the survivor’s agreement (except anonymous submissions).

7.5.1.5          Adhere to Survivor Protection & Non-Retaliation
No retaliation, punishment, or adverse consequences may occur toward complainants, witnesses, or service providers.
Any retaliation (by contractors, workers, community members, or officials) is treated as a separate SEA/SH violation
requiring immediate action. SEA/SH survivors are not required to provide evidence, participate in mediation, or
confront perpetrators.



Kambarata-1 HPP                                                  Official Use Only   Client Reference No. 5018007
P21: Stakeholder Engagement Plan & Grievance Redress Mechanism                       SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
                                                                                     25 February 2026                               74
MONITORING AND REPORTING

7.5.1.6          Requirements of contractors and subcontractors
Asa minimum all contractors and sub-contractors will:
      •   Appoint a SEA/SH Officer,

      •   Ensure all workers sign a SEA/SH Code of Conduct.

      •   Conduct mandatory induction and refresher training.

      •   Report SEA/SH allegations to K1-PMO within 24 hours.

      •   Take immediate protective measures, including removal of the accused from the worksite.




7.6              Monitoring and reporting
The K1-PMO will establish a system to monitor the functioning, accessibility, timeliness, and effectiveness of the
grievance management system. This will involve regular follow-ups with complainants to assess their satisfaction with
the grievance resolution process and the outcomes. The K1-PMO will conduct periodic surveys and feedback sessions
to gather insights on the effectiveness and fairness of the grievance redress mechanism. The feedback collected will be
analyzed to identify areas for improvement and ensure that the grievance redress process is responsive to the needs
and concerns of the community.
The K1-PMO will also ensure that all feedback is documented and used to enhance the overall effectiveness of the
grievance management system. The K1-PMO responsible staff (Social Specialist and M&E Specialist) will monitor the
timeframes for reviewing grievances at all levels. If additional technical review or verification is required, including the
involvement of relevant third parties, the review timeframes may be reasonably extended. If there is a justified need
and the deadlines for considering grievances and appeals are extended, the PMO staff assigned to the specific grievance
will prepare summary information and make appropriate notes in the unified GRM Log.
The K1-PMO will maintain internal monthly grievance monitoring records and as part of agreed E&S performance
reporting arrangements.The report shall include the number of grievances at both the local and central levels, along
with the number of grievances resolved and ongoing, categorized by GRM categories and levels. Additionally, the PMO
shall prepare and submit quarterly reports for review by the Bank as part of the E&S risk management performance
reports.. The overall effectiveness of the grievance management procedure, including accessibility, timeliness,
transparency, and resolution rates, will be assessed semi-annually by the project management.
Reporting will be based on aggregated and non-identifiable data. No personal or case-specific information, particularly
related to SEA/SH grievances, will be disclosed.

7.7              World Bank Redress Grievances Services
Communities and individuals who believe they have been affected by a World Bank-supported project can submit
complaints using the existing project-level grievance redress mechanisms or the World Bank Grievance Redress Service
(GRS). The GRS ensures that grievances received are dealt with in a timely manner to resolve any project-related issues.
Affected communities and individuals may submit a complaint to the World Bank's Independent Inspection Panel, which
examines allegations that the World Bank has not complied with its policies and procedures and whether such non-
compliance may have caused harm.
Information on how to submit a complaint to the World Bank’s corporate Grievance Redress Service (GRS) is available
on the World Bank website: http://www.worldbank.org/en/projects-operations/products-and-services/grievance-
redressservice.

8.               MONITORING AND REPORTING
The stakeholder engagement activities will form part of the Environmental and Social Commitment Plan (ESCP). The
ESCP is a tool through which the Borrower commits to implementing the agreed environmental and social measures
and actions, including stakeholder engagement activities, and forms part of the project approval documentation
monitored by the World Bank.
The project monitoring and evaluation framework will include indicators related to ESCP implementation, and progress
will be tracked and reported accordingly. ESCP implementation progress reports will be periodically produced and
shared with the World Bank and relevant stakeholders, as appropriate, in accordance with disclosure requirements.
Monitoring and evaluation of the stakeholder engagement process is essential for the K1-PMO to respond to emerging
issues and, where necessary, adjust the timing, scope, or methods of engagement to improve effectiveness. Monitoring
of stakeholder engagement will focus on process indicators and selected outcome indicators relevant to ESS10, without
duplicating monitoring undertaken under other environmental and social instruments.
Compliance with the following commitments will support effective stakeholder engagement:
•         Sufficient resources allocated for implementation;
•         Inclusive engagement of key stakeholder groups, including vulnerable groups;
•         Adequate facilitation of stakeholder engagement activities;
•         Clearly defined engagement approaches; and
Transparency in all engagement and reporting activities.

8.1              Monitoring Approach and Monitoring Indicators
This SEP will be periodically revised and updated as necessary in the course of the project implementation in order to
ensure that the information presented herein is consistent and is the most recent, and that the identified methods of
engagement remain appropriate and effective in relation to the project context and specific phases of the development.
Any major changes to the project related activities and to its schedule will be duly reflected in the SEP.
Monthly internal summaries on grievances and stakeholder feedback will be prepared for internal management
purposes. Consolidated quarterly reports will be used for external reporting, including reporting to the World Bank..
The monthly summaries will provide a mechanism for assessing both the number and the nature of complaints and
requests for information, along with the Project’s ability to address those in a timely and satisfactorily manner.
Stakeholder engagement will be monitored through two complementary approaches: (i) ongoing monitoring during
engagement activities to ensure effective implementation and allow timely corrective actions; and (ii) periodic
evaluation following engagement phases to assess effectiveness, inclusivity, and integration of stakeholder feedback
into project decision-making.
      •   Short-Term Monitoring is conducted during engagement activities to ensure effective delivery, identify
          immediate issues, and enable real-time adjustments. Tools include participation tracking, brief feedback
          surveys, and rapid-response mechanisms.
      •   Post-Engagement Evaluation occurs after the completion of engagement phases to assess overall effectiveness,
          inclusivity, and integration of stakeholder input. Methods include outcome-based KPI analysis, stakeholder
          interviews, and including reviews with attention to gender and social inclusion aspects, consistent with ESS10..
      •   Monitoring will be consistent with ESS10 requirements on inclusive engagement, with specific attention to
          gender-disaggregated participation data and the participation of vulnerable groups.
This dual approach supports both operational responsiveness and strategic learning, ensuring engagement remains
adaptive, accountable, and aligned with project and stakeholder needs.
The following KPIs will be used to monitor the effectiveness of the SEP:

Stakeholder comments and concerns raised during engagement activities are tracked through consultation records and
formal response matrices. Implementation of engagement-related commitments is monitored through SEP indicators,
while technical issues are monitored through corresponding environmental and social instruments.

8.2              Monitoring Reports During Construction
Internal Reporting
Throughout the construction phase, K1-PMO will undertake regular internal monitoring of stakeholder engagement and
broader E&S performance. Key activities include:
      •   Monthly internal summaries: During the construction phase, brief monthly summaries will be prepared by the
          responsible staff, capturing key elements of the SEP implementation, including indicators listed in Table 8-1.
          These summaries will consolidate data on public grievances, enquiries, and other stakeholder feedback,
          alongside the status of related corrective actions. The summaries will serve as an important tool for evaluating
          the volume, nature, and resolution of stakeholder concerns, and will be escalated to senior management for
          review and response.
      •   Quarterly and annual internal management reports: Monthly summaries will inform the preparation of more
          comprehensive quarterly and annual internal reports, which will include an analysis of trends, lessons learned,
          and overall effectiveness of engagement practices. These reports will support internal decision-making and
          course correction, where needed.
External Reporting
Quarterly E&S reports will be prepared and submitted to the World Bank during the construction period. A section on
stakeholder engagement will be included in these reports which will include an update on implementation of the
stakeholder engagement plan and include indicators in Table 8-1.

8.3              Monitoring Reports during Operation
Internal Reporting
During the first three years of operation, six-monthly E&S compliance reports will be prepared, complemented by
annual consolidated E&S reports. Monthly internal summaries are management tools and do not constitute formal
external reporting.
External Reporting
During the initial years of operation, subject to refinement of operational arrangement, six-monthly and annual E&S
compliance reports will be prepared and submitted to the World Bank. Annual E&S compliance reports will then be
prepared and submitted to the World Bank during the rest of the operation period. A section on stakeholder
engagement will be included in these E&S compliance reports. In addition, K1-PMO will prepare Incident Notifications
for the World Bank, if and when, required.

8.4              Involvement of stake holders in monitoring activities
The Project will provide several opportunities to stakeholders, especially Project Affected Parties to monitor certain
aspects of Project performance, within the scope of stakeholder engagement, grievance redress, and community-level
feedback mechanisms, and citizen/PAP surveys.. The Project GRM will allow PAPs to submit grievances and other types


Kambarata-1 HPP                                                  Official Use Only   Client Reference No. 5018007
P21: Stakeholder Engagement Plan & Grievance Redress Mechanism                       SMEC Internal Ref. 5018007
                                                                                     25 February 2026                           78
MONITORING AND REPORTING

of feedback. Citizen/PAP surveys at the project mid-point and end stages will also allow PAPs to provide feedback on
project performance. Furthermore, frequent and regular community meetings and interactions with K1-PMO staff,
especially local NGOs, will allow PAPs and other local stakeholders to be heard and engaged

8.5              Reporting Back to Stakeholders
The project performance assessed through monitoring activities will be reported back to stakeholders during the
operation and maintenance phase, such as through disclosure of monitoring outcome and engagement with local
community. The lessons learned through the monitoring will also contribute to the design of future subprojects and be
shared with their stakeholders.
Monitoring and evaluation of the stakeholder process is considered vital to ensure the project is able to respond to
identified issues and alter the schedule and nature of engagement activities to make them more effective. Adherence
to the following characteristics/ commitments/activities will assist in achieving successful engagement:
      •   Sufficient resources to undertake the engagement;
      •   Inclusivity (inclusion of key groups) of interactions with stakeholders;
      •   Promotion of stakeholder involvement;
      •   Sense of trust shown by all stakeholders;
      •   Clearly defined approaches; and
      •   Transparency in all activities.

A-1-1           National Consultations (Bishkek) of the Ecological and Social Study Technical Assistance
                for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project Meeting Minutes
Date and Time                             : 19.11.2024
Organizer                                 : Ministry of Energy of the Kyrgyz Republic
Location                                  : Bishkek, Kyrgyz Republic

TOPIC/OBJECTIVE
Conducting the Environmental and Social Assessment. Initial National Consultations.
The Purpose of the Consultation is letting all interested parties (governmental, non-governmental, institutions, and non-
profit organizations) ask questions, express their concerns, and provide suggestions on the Project.

SUMMARY OF INFORMATION PRESENTED
The Consultants presented the Presentation and opened the Q&A session. The facilitator directed raised questions to
the responsible parties to answer. The Consultation included all project-related aspects, including environmental and
social, legal, technical, compensation, management, and communication matters. Suggested proposals and feedback
from participants were taken into consideration. Some questions must be answered upon completing the survey/steps
by the next Consultation Session. The languages of conduct for the Consultation are English, Russian, and Kyrgyz.
Simultaneous translation was provided.

SUMMARY OF PARTICIPANTS
A total of 35 participants including 9 NGO representatives took part in the consultations. Females accounted for 22% of
the total participants. Participants also included local and international NGOs, various Institutional groups, from local
media, research educational bodies such as Association of Women in Energy, environmental coalition, district level
regulatory bodies (Toktogul district akim), various ministries such as National Institute of Public Health of the Ministry
of Health, Ministry of Emergency Situations and different individual from epidemic background such as pollical scientist
etc.
Invitations were sent in advance via email and official letter from the Ministry of Energy, allowing ample time for
international NGOs to arrange their travel. Special invitations were also extended to representatives of Civil Society to
ensure their active participation.
Online participation was also organized for those interested stakeholders. The link to the event was provided in advance.
All necessary efforts were made to ensure that the consultations were inclusive, taking into account the diverse interests
and needs.

A-1-2           Local Consultations (Nichke-Say village, Toktogul district) of the Ecological and Social
                Study Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydro-power Plant Project Meeting
                Minutes
Date and Time        : October 22, 2024 14:00
Organizer            : K1 HPP PMO, with support from SMEC
Location : Nichke-Say village, the former building of Uturov Z.N
TOPIC/OBJECTIVES
The objective of the meeting is to provide information on the goals and current activities of the Kambar-Ata-1 HPP
project. According to the Kyrgyz Republic’s regulations and international standards, a detailed Environmental and Social
Impact Assessment (ESIA) must be conducted to assess and mitigation project’s risks and impacts, in addition to address
specific concerns of local communities. Consultations with stakeholders interested in the project are essential for
gaining a deeper understanding of their interests and concerns.
Concerns of local residents and communities will be considered seriously while carrying out the ESIA. So the primary
purpose of the meeting is to ensure transparency in project planning and implementation.
SUMMARY OF INFORMATION PRESENTED
E.S. Mukhambetov, Head of the Department of the Ministry of Energy of the Kyrgyz Republic, presented on the Kambar-
Ata-1 HPP project. The main points discussed were as follows,
    •     Presentation of the Kambar-Ata-1 HPP project.
    •     Construction will be carried out with the support of the World Bank.
    •     The planned implementation period is 6.5 years.
    •     HPP capacity - 5 billion kWh.
    •     Creation of 3,000 jobs with priority hiring of residents.
    •     Average salary - 50,000 Soms.
    •     Prepare infrastructure: concrete dam, new roads, bridges and substation.
    •     The contractor is SMEC, an Australian company engaged in environmental and social assessment of the project.
SUMMARY OF PARTICIPANTS
A total of 44 participants took part in the consultations. Females accounted for 25% of the total participants. Participants
also included local authorities, community organizations, projects affected people, including the vulnerable groups.
Invitations were sent through local government authorities, community organizations, as well as WhatsApp Messenger.
Meetings were held with Ayil Okmotu (Local authority) to inform and engage participants, especially project affected
people. Special invitations were extended to project affected people to ensure their active participation.
All necessary efforts were made to ensure that the consultations were inclusive, taking into account the diverse interests
and needs.

A-1-3           Local Consultations (Toluk a/a, Toktogul district) of the Ecological and Social Study
                Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydropower Plant Project Meeting Minutes

Date and Time: October 23, 2024, 12:00 PM
Organizer:   K1 HPP PMO, with support from SMEC
Location:   Toluk, House of Culture, named after S. Nasyrkanov

TOPIC/OBJECTIVE
The objective of the meeting is to provide information on the goals and current activities of the Kambar-Ata-1 HPP
project. According to the Kyrgyz Republic’s regulations and international standards, a detailed Environmental and Social
Impact Assessment (ESIA) must be conducted to assess and mitigation project’s risks and impacts, in addition to address
specific concerns of local communities. The ESIA will evaluate the project's potential impacts and propose mitigation
measures.
The Ministry of Energy and its potential international partners view consultations with stakeholders interested in the
project as essential for gaining a deeper understanding of their interests and concerns.
Concerns of local residents and communities will be considered seriously while carrying out the ESIA.
The primary purpose of the meeting is to ensure transparency in project planning and implementation.

SUMMARY OF INFORMATION PRESENTED
The head of the Village Toluk provided a brief overview of previous meetings related to the Kambar-Ata-1 HPP project.
Then, he highlighted the key issues: clarifying boundaries, assessing flooded land, and discussing compensation.
A representative of the Ministry of Energy emphasized the importance of the Kambar-Ata-1 HPP project as a national
flagship and reported on $5 million in research funding from the World Bank. The representative explained the project's
impact on the lands of the Toluk, Nichke-Sai, and Sary-Kamysh regions and reported on the role of international partners
(Uzbekistan and Kazakhstan) in the project. The representative also answered residents' questions regarding timing,
compensation, and environmental impacts.
The representative of SMEC company reported on SMEC's activities within the environmental and social assessment.
The World Bank standards in the projects were also outlined. The grievance redress mechanism with local communities
was presented.
SUMMARY OF PARTICIPANTS
A total of 67 participants took part in the consultations. Females accounted for 2,9% of the total participants.
Participants also included local authorities, community organizations, projects affected people.
Invitations were sent through local government authorities, community organizations, as well as Whats App Messenger.
Meetings were held with Ayil Okmotu (Local authority) to inform and engage participants, especially project affected
people. Special invitations were extended to project affected people to ensure their active participation.

A-1-6           Local Consultations (Sary-Kamysh ayil aimak, Toktogul district) of the Ecological and
                Social Study Technical Assistance for Kambarata 1 Hydro-Power Plant Project Meeting
                Minutes.


Date and Time                  : October 30, 2024 11:30-14:00
Organized                      : K1 HPP PMO, with support from SMEC
Location             : Sary Kamysh village, Sary Kamysh Ayil Aimak, Toktogul district


TOPIC/OBJECTIVE
The objective of the meeting is to provide information on the goals and current activities of the Kambar-Ata-1 HPP
project. According to the Kyrgyz Republic’s regulations and international standards, a detailed Environmental and Social
Impact Assessment (ESIA) must be conducted to assess and mitigation project’s risks and impacts, in addition to address
specific concerns of local communities.
Consultations with stakeholders interested in the project are essential for gaining a deeper understanding of their
interests and concerns.
Concerns of local residents and communities will be considered seriously while carrying out the ESIA. The primary
purpose of the meeting is to ensure transparency in project planning and implementation.
SUMMARY OF INFORMATION PRESENTED
E.S. Mukhambetov, Head of the Department of the Ministry of Energy of the Kyrgyz Republic, presented on the Kambar-
Ata-1 HPP project. The main points of the report:
    •      Presentation of the Kambar-Ata-1 HPP project.
    •      Construction will be carried out with the support of the World Bank.
    •      The planned implementation period is 6.5 years.
    •      HPP capacity - 5 billion kWh.
    •      Creation of 3,000 jobs with priority hiring of local residents.
    •      Average salary - 50,000 soms.
    •      Preparation of infrastructure: concrete dam, new roads, bridges and substation.
    •      The contractor is SMEC, an Australian company engaged in environmental and social assessment of the project.
To improve the accuracy of the survey, maps of the flooded areas have been prepared. Residents have asked that all
objects be marked on them: bridges, trails, huts and other important objects for the population.
SUMMARY OF PARTICIPANT
A total of 50 participants took part in the consultations. Females accounted for 20% of the total participants. Participants
also included local authorities, community organizations, projects affected people, including the vulnerable groups.
Invitations were sent through local government authorities, community organizations, as well as WhatsApp Messenger.
Meetings were held with Ayil Okmotu (Local authority) to inform and engage participants, especially project affected
people. Special invitations were extended to project affected people to ensure their active participation.
All necessary efforts were made to ensure that the consultations were inclusive, taking into account the diverse interests
and needs.




FEEDBACK RECEIVED




 P509251-1cf198a7-34a9-44bd-a9ad-c4249a506373.docx                                      Official Use Only        Page 97 of 182
Kambarata-1 HPP




 Who                          Description / Feedback/Inputs

 Raised by the                Q: Reconstruction of K1 HPP has its roots since the Soviet era, and of course, it requires a
 participant, O.T.            very careful approach. Research should be conducted, and the concerns of the people must
                              be taken into account. Our people have lived here for many years, primarily engaged in
                              cattle breeding. It is our only source of income. We are not traders and are not adapted to
                              market-based relations. Our community is very traditional and adheres to its customs. For
                              example, according to our traditions, we do not sell dairy products like milk, kumis, etc. We
                              can offer it to quests, but we do not sell. As you can see, we live in harsh conditions,
                              situated between two mountains. In winter, the roads are completely closed. We keep all
                              our livestock on the pastures that are in the flooding zone. These lands are among the most
                              important for the livelihood of our population.
                              Of course, we are not against the construction of the Kambar-Ata HPP. We completely
                              understand the meaning of the project for the country. However, the welfare of the people
                              must also be considered, and conditions for their livelihood need to be created. We have
                              about 700 small livestock and over 1,000 large cattle.
                              We do have other pasture lands such as Chilten and Sary-Bulak, which could serve as
                              replacements for the lands that will be flooded. The problem is that these areas lack roads,
                              and it is impossible to reach them. It would be helpful to consider the possibility of building
                              roads to these pastures. These lands play a crucial role in the lives of our people, who are
                              known for their hard work.
                              There is also a bridge in the Min-Zhylky section, and according to our information, it is also
                              within the flooding zone. So, we may not be able to access that area either. This is our first
                              concern.
                              The second issue is that the residents of our village have built a 9 km road with their own
                              efforts and resources, which leads to the main highway. Every household contributed both
                              financially and physically to the construction of this road. During the winter, we are
                              completely isolated, and it is impossible to use the existing roads due to safety concerns. As
                              a result, the people have taken it upon themselves to build this road with their own means.
                              We would like to appeal to the government to take this road into its official balance, check
                              it for compliance with standards, and ensure it is fit for operation.
                              And thirdly, perhaps the possibility of allocating land plots for young families should be
                              considered. The government should care about its people and think about their well-being.
                              There should be no attitude that people can live in such conditions, with their needs
                              ignored, or even worsened in some areas. Therefore, we all hope for a fair resolution to this
                              issue. Everyone currently involved in this project should also keep in mind the importance
                              of helping to implement the project and assist the population that is losing lands vital to
                              their livelihoods. In ten years, when you meet the residents of these villages, we hope they
                              will remember you kindly.

 Raised by the                Q: Experts like you also visited back in 2014. We have shared all our concerns with them.
 participant, I.D.            First of all, you should take to your mind that people make their living through livestock.
                              Our village is located at the foot of the mountain. Our pasture for grazing livestock is far
                              away. Our roads are in poor condition and feeding the animals is a great challenge for us.
                              For this reason, we keep all our livestock in the winter pastures. The area that will be
                              flooded is where our winter pastures are located.
                              In addition, we also have salt deposits there. We extract salt from this area, but it will also
                              be impounded. We, of course, do not intend to be against the Project. Definitely no. We
                              support this project. However, we ask that the government also considers our situation.
                              Our pastures are shrinking. For example, even the Zhumgal district is disputing our pasture
                              lands. Therefore, if our pastures are going to be impounded, it would be helpful if roads
                              could be built to alternative pastures.

Session – Questions and Answers
Emil: Good afternoon, participants, as well as online participants. We have received many questions on various topics.
Given that the AFRY specialists are located in Southeast Asia and there is a significant time difference, we will first
address the questions related to dam safety and seismology associated with these issues. Let me immediately raise the
question we received from Ibragimjan Tomuladzhanov.




 P509251-1cf198a7-34a9-44bd-a9ad-c4249a506373.docx                                        Official Use Only        Page 106 of 182
Kambarata-1 HPP




The first question: Have issues of an accident during an earthquake been taken into account?
The seismicity of this area is 8–9 on the Richter scale. What consequences can this lead to?
Denis, please, this question is for you.
Answer (Denis, AFRY): Regarding emergency situations, all of them are analyzed in the emergency response plans that
are currently being developed. AFRY has already submitted the first draft. The Ministry has received it, and one of the
topics concerns earthquakes: the magnitude of earthquakes, their impact on the dam, and the response procedures.
These emergency plans also describe monitoring measures and distribution of responsibilities — who is responsible for
what. These reports are being prepared independently of the Feasibility Study (FS). Furthermore, as we have already
mentioned, the FS is only the first stage. The impact of earthquakes on the dam will be additionally modeled at the
stage of the technical design and at the stage of the detailed design. When, in the coming years, more information is
collected — including from the new network of seismic sensors that is already being installed at the future project site
— the assessment will be refined. This is one of the first steps already being implemented now — the installation of the
seismic sensor network. At this stage, this is the maximum that can be said on this question.
Question: How is water diversion / water supply managed during construction?
Answer (Denis, AFRY): Okay. To explain this more clearly, I will share my screen. In our FS, the main stages related to
how water intake and diversion are organized and how the dam is constructed are highlighted. This can be conditionally
divided into four main stages. The first stage involves earthworks on the right and left riverbanks. In parallel, excavation
is carried out in the area where the upper and lower cofferdams will be located. At the second stage, the upper
cofferdam is built, and in parallel, the diversion tunnels are constructed. When both the cofferdam and the diversion
tunnels are completed, the river channel is redirected through these tunnels. This allows for the construction of the
lower cofferdam. In the diagram you can see that both cofferdams are already in place, and the river is flowing through
the tunnels. This ensures that the entire area where the future dam will be located remains dry. In this area, construction
and preparatory works are carried out: foundation cleaning, excavation, and site preparation. At the end, the dam itself
is constructed from roller-compacted concrete with all integrated structures. In the last stage, shown in the illustration,
the cofferdam is still in place, but the dam is already built. When all conditions are met — the lower and middle spillways
are built and ready for operation — reservoir filling may begin. Regarding filling, another slide shows the graph of
average river flows by month. The filling speed of the reservoir depends on the hydrological year in which the filling will
take place, and on the month when the diversion tunnels stop operating and filling begins. If filling starts, as shown in
the graph, approximately in April, then on average the entire reservoir can be filled within two and a half to three
months. If filling begins only at the end of summer or the beginning of autumn (for example, in September), this process
may take five or even seven months — everything depends on the hydrological year.
Next question: Can seismic activity be related to solar activity?
Answer (Stefan, Seismic Expert): Stefan, this is regarding the question: can seismic activity be related to solar activity?
Maybe you can answer this question.
Stefan: Let me answer. The general approach that we use in our seismic studies is recognized and widely accepted
internationally, published in many scientific works and applied in many projects. There are various innovations, but it is
based on tectonics, Earth sciences, geology, and the analysis of past earthquakes. We consider faults, geological
structures, trenching results, and other aspects.
Addition (Denis, translation): I will just add that, as Stefan said, according to international practice, there is no direct
proven correlation between seismic activity and solar activity. Thank you.
Next question: Is there a clear evacuation plan and an alert system for the population in case of potential risks during
construction and after?
Emil: Hello, participants, as well as online participants. We have received many questions on various topics. Considering
that AFRY specialists are located in Southeast Asia and there is a significant time difference, we will first review the
questions related to dam safety and seismology. I will immediately ask the question that we received from Ibragimjan
Tomuladzhanov. The first question: Have earthquake-related accident scenarios been considered? The seismicity of
this area is 8–9 on the Richter scale. What consequences can this lead to? Denis, please, this question is for you.
Answer (Denis, AFRY):
Regarding emergency situations, all of them are analyzed in the Emergency Preparedness Plans that are currently being
developed. AFRY has already submitted the first draft. The Ministry has received it, and one of the questions addressed
in the plan concerns earthquakes: earthquake magnitude, impacts on the dam, and the response procedures. These
emergency plans also describe monitoring measures and the distribution of responsibilities—who is responsible for
what. These reports are being prepared independently from the Feasibility Study (FS). As we have already mentioned,




 P509251-1cf198a7-34a9-44bd-a9ad-c4249a506373.docx                                   Official Use Only          Page 107 of 182
Kambarata-1 HPP



the FS is only the first stage. The impact of earthquakes on the dam will be additionally modelled at the technical design
stage and at the detailed design stage.
When more information is collected in the coming years, including from the new seismic sensor network that is already
being installed at the project site, the assessment will be refined. This installation of seismic sensors is one of the first
steps that is already being implemented now. At this stage, this is the maximum that can be said regarding this question.
Next question: The flow data were provided by the Hydrometeorological Agency under the Ministry of Emergency
Situations, commissioned by the Ministry of Energy. These data were then forwarded for processing, analysis, and
modelling. These data are based on observations collected over past decades. Do you confirm this?
Answer: Yes, we confirm. I can only add, if my screen is still visible. As already mentioned, all data were officially
requested. We requested daily data, and the state institution—the Hydrometeorological Center—kindly provided them.
A large amount of data was collected. Mainly, there are three types of data. First – precipitation. Here you can see 10
stations in the Naryn Basin that were the sources of precipitation data. Second type – air temperature. On the map, all
stations that measure air temperature in the Naryn Basin are shown; their data were also collected. Third type – river
discharge, measured at stations in the basin equipped with flow-measurement instruments. For all these parameters,
multi-year daily average data were provided. This complete dataset, officially obtained from the Kyrgyz
Hydrometeorological Service, created the basis for our hydrological model and further simulations related to electricity
generation. Thank you.

water level starts to rise. River banks about 80 km long fall into the zone of changing conditions, and this has been taken
into account.

significantly further downstream and outside the influence zone of K-1. Therefore, the impact on water users below the
dam is zero.

First question from Ibrahimdzhan Domuladzhanov: Have the issues related to the fact that the seismicity of this area
is 8 on the Richter scale been taken into account? What consequences can this lead to?
Danius: As for emergencies, all of them are currently being analysed in emergency action plans that are being developed
separately. AFRY has already submitted the first draft to the Ministry. And one of the key issues there is precisely
earthquakes: their magnitude, their impact on the dam and how to respond to such events. These emergency plans also
spell out monitoring measures and the allocation of responsibilities — who is responsible for what. These reports are
being prepared independently of the Feasibility Study. Further, at the next stages, as we have already said, the Feasibility
Study is only the first stage; the impact of earthquakes on the dam will be modelled in detail within the framework of
the technical design, at the stage of the basic (technical) design and then at the stage of detailed design. When more
information is collected over the coming years — and one of the first tasks, which is already being implemented now, is
the installation of a seismic sensor network at the future project site — these data will be used to refine the calculations.
So at this stage this is the most complete answer possible.
Emil: The next question from Anna Rychkov: How is water intake from the reservoir and its filling carried out?
Danius: To explain this more clearly, I will share my screen. Basically, in our Feasibility Study project we have already
identified the main stages related to how water is taken, how construction is carried out and how the reservoir is filled.
This can be conditionally divided into four key stages. The first stage is earthworks in the area of the right and left banks.
In parallel, excavation is carried out in the riverbed at the locations where the upper and lower cofferdams will be
arranged — if you can see my cursor, we are talking about the upper cofferdam upstream and the lower cofferdam
downstream of the dam.At the second stage, the upper cofferdam is being constructed, and at the same time the
diversion tunnels must be driven. When both the cofferdam and the diversion tunnels are built, the river is temporarily
diverted into these tunnels. After that, the lower cofferdam can be arranged. In the drawing presented you can see both
cofferdams, and the river is already flowing through the tunnels. In this way, the entire construction zone of the future
dam is dewatered. In this dry zone, construction and preparatory works are carried out: cleaning, soil excavation,
preparation of the foundation. In the final stage, the dam itself is built from roller-compacted concrete with all
integrated structures. In the last stage that you see in the diagram, the cofferdam is still in place, but the dam has
already been built. When all necessary conditions are met — and there are many of them: the lower and middle
spillways must be ready and operable — only after that can the filling of the reservoir begin. As for the filling process
itself, on this slide you can see graphs of the river’s average monthly flow. The rate of filling depends on the hydrological
year in which filling will be carried out and on the month when the diversion tunnels are closed and water starts to be
stored in the reservoir. If, as seen from the graph, filling begins, say, in April, then on average the entire reservoir can
be filled in about two and a half to three months. If filling starts only at the end of summer, in September or early
autumn, this process can take five or even seven months — depending on how wet the year is. I think this answers your
question at the current stage.

were provided by Hydromet on the basis of observations they carry out — they have been collecting data for

Danius: Yes, we confirm this. I can add. If you see my screen — this is a map. As has already been said, all data were
officially requested, and we were given daily data. The state institution — the Hydrometeorological Centre — kindly
provided all these data. A lot of data were collected, but basically they are of three types:
• the first is precipitation;
• the second is air temperature;
• the third is river discharge, measured at gauging stations.

All three types of data were provided as daily average values. If you look at the map, you can see about ten stations in

Next question from Anna Rychkova: Can seismic activity be related to solar activity?
Stefan: In general terms, the approach we use in the seismic study is a commonly accepted, internationally recognized
approach. It is based on tectonics, earth sciences, geology and the analysis of past earthquakes. Faults, their geometry,
characteristics, historical seismicity, and so on are considered.
Danius: I would just add that, as Stefan said, in international practice there is no confirmed direct correlation between
seismic activity and solar activity.
Emil: Another question: is there a clear evacuation plan and an alert system for the population living downstream in the

Danius: As for evacuation, I already mentioned that preliminary plans are currently being developed. Under the World
Bank’s methodology, the Ministry commissioned such a plan, and we have prepared the first step — preliminary plans
for evacuation in case of emergencies. These plans include, among other things, what the responsible organization will
do if something goes wrong with the dam, or if, as a result of an earthquake or according to instrument readings, alarm
signals appear. In such a case, the areas located downstream of the dam, including settlements, are promptly informed.
All        this         is       now           being         considered         at          a        preliminary        level.
It is important to stress once again that this is only the first step at the Feasibility Study stage. At the next stage — the
technical design — a dam break consequences analysis must be carried out in parallel. As part of this analysis, the
reports will present maps of possible inundation areas in an extreme case. Based on these maps, the theoretical
downstream flooding impact will be assessed and warning signals and procedures on how to prepare for such situations
will be developed. This will then become the subject of more detailed work at the subsequent stages.
Emil: Another question from Alexander Strom. He notes that the seismic assessments use values that do not take into
account such factors as topographic amplification, whereas it can be significant. In addition, according to him, the entire
dam construction site is located on the hanging wall, very close to a fault, and the chosen location for the dam may not
be optimal for such a high dam. He then gives an example of a possible alternative alignment further upstream, which
in his opinion is more symmetrical and farther from the fault.
Stefan: In this question we can distinguish two aspects, and it is more logical to answer them separately. The first aspect
concerns possible fault displacements. As I have already noted, we have studied numerous faults in the area of the
proposed project and assessed the possible magnitudes of displacement. We understand well that there is considerable
uncertainty here: this is not an exact science. Displacement estimates are generally based on empirical formulas.
Nevertheless, we took into account all the main faults around the future dam, relying on tectonics and geological data.
We do not expect multi-metre displacements directly at the dam axis. As I mentioned in the presentation, we also
performed a failure mode analysis and considered many possible scenarios associated with fault displacements. These
studies are carried out to identify risks and mitigation measures. If necessary, I can repeat this part. Overall, despite the
existing uncertainties, the displacement assessment has been carried out correctly, taking into account geological
conditions, the direction and magnitude of potential displacements, and the nature of movement on the main faults.
Now I will move to the second part of the question — topographic amplification. This effect must be taken into account
either within the seismic study itself or at later stages during the dynamic analysis of the dam. The acceleration time
histories obtained as a result of seismic calculations are fed into numerical models. At the current stage, a pseudo-
dynamic analysis has been performed, and at the next stage a full three-dimensional dynamic modelling will be carried
out. In the course of this analysis, the effects of amplification of accelerations on slopes, on the dam crest and at its
abutments will be assessed. Correct consideration of the site topography will be continued. I hope at this stage this
provides a satisfactory answer.
Additionally, I note that we are now at the Feasibility Study stage. The next stage is the development of the detailed
design. Before starting detailed design, a set of additional investigations is planned — geological, geophysical, seismic
— focused on those areas for which more accurate data are needed. Within the seismic investigations, a micro seismic
network will be installed covering the entire area and allowing us to obtain accurate data on topographic amplification.
Seismic works will also be carried out: drilling of boreholes, measurements of wave velocities in the boreholes and
surface wave surveys. These additional data will be used to refine calculations and models where necessary. This fully
answers the question that was raised earlier: the assessment at the Feasibility Study stage is carried out on the basis of




 P509251-1cf198a7-34a9-44bd-a9ad-c4249a506373.docx                                    Official Use Only           Page 110 of 182
Kambarata-1 HPP



the information available, and further field investigations will make it possible to refine and improve the accuracy of
calculations.
Liliana: I can add a little that this is normal international practice: at the Feasibility Study stage a roadmap for further
investigations is formed; at the basic design stage the information is supplemented and refined; even after the start of
construction the project remains adaptive. Engineering optimization continues constantly — from the Feasibility Study
to the basic design, then to the detailed design, and further, taking into account new data obtained directly on site.
Danius: We fully agree with this approach and confirm that the steps presented correspond to international engineering

Alexander Strom: You see, I have worked at “Hydroproject” for 50 years and I know very well all the stages of design

Danius: I can answer. Thank you for voicing your concern. I do not know whether you had the opportunity to read our
report to the end or not. But at the Feasibility Study stage, one dam type, one scheme, one alignment is always chosen
— that is the very essence of a feasibility study. The question cannot be left completely open; one option must be
selected for further development. But this does not mean that nothing can be changed at subsequent stages. As we
have already said, when moving to the next stage and then to the third stage, there is always flexibility to take into
account additional information. As for alternatives, we analysed the materials of the 2014 Feasibility Study. Based on
the current information on the dam site, for the proposed alignment we already have some geological investigations,
digital topographic survey and everything that could be gathered at this point. If we talk about moving the dam axis
upstream, the question arises: on what information will we rely there? This is not only geology and seismics, but also
topography. For other Soviet-era options there was no proper digital topography — scanned maps were used, without
georeferencing, Soviet topographic plans at scales of about 1:10,000. But this is not the level of accuracy that can be
used for modern calculations of such a facility. For the current alignment we have modern digital data and additional
investigations. If we move the alignment, then at the new location the volume of data is much smaller, and from the
Feasibility Study point of view this may even be less substantiated than the current option.
Danius: Do you agree?
Aleksandr Strom: No, I do not agree.
Danius: Where do you get your topography from, then?
Danius: In Soviet times, a project at the level of a feasibility study was carried out, with options for a high-head dam

Emil: Kyrgyzstan has signed the Convention on Transboundary Watercourses. How does it relate to the Kambarata-1

Rysbekov Altynbek Durusbekovich: My colleague has already correctly noted that political and intergovernmental

calculations and the inter-seasonal water regime, which is related specifically to the operation of the hydropower
complex. I would also like to remind you that water supply issues have been discussed by the countries in the past.
Between Uzbekistan, Kyrgyzstan and Kazakhstan, agreements were concluded in 1996–1998. Under these
arrangements, Kyrgyzstan provided the required volume of water for the downstream countries — Uzbekistan and
Kazakhstan — and in return these countries provided supplies of natural gas, oil products and other resources. These
